==PAGE:5==
﻿
V
Abstract
The increasing awareness of our impact on the world has driven the 
need for designing buildings with a sustainable approach more than 
ever before. However, using building sustainability assessment tools, 
such as DGNB (Deutsche Gesellschaft für Nachhaltiges Bauen), in­
volves comprehensive, complex and time-consuming activity during a 
design process. This has led to numerous attempts to optimise the de­
sign process from a management perspective or by employing a mul­
ti-criteria decision-making approach to the use of sustainability tools.
This study takes a holistic qualitative approach to the aim of mapping 
the structure of DGNB Office Building and finding key areas that 
needs special focus during a design process, if a high level of sustain­
ability is to be achieved. Through interviews participants were asked 
which areas they found important during a design process, scoping 
from the project brief to the construction of the building. The results 
from the interviews were analysed by the use of Grounded Theory, a 
systematic methodology used in the social sciences.
The research showed that it is critical to address certain elements. 
The client brief must define a healthy framework through specific 
requirements to both collaboration and project, ideally taken from 
DGNB. The client and the design team should together define a key 
set of criteria that was important for the client and afterwards set a 
separate aim of how the remaining points should be achieved. The key 
criteria set was found to often be in line with a company’s CSR-pro­
file. Bigger criteria, such as LCA and LCC, were important due to 
their size and interrelations with other building aspects. Hence they 
had to be addressed iteratively throughout the different phases. It 
was important to maintain a holistic perspective, and DGNB should 
be addressed qualitatively in the early phases. The aesthetical quality 
struggled getting its importance defined by DGNB, which meant the 
design team had to be careful with a cold-hearted race for points in 
DGNB score. DGNB was also found to have affect the architecture, 
leading to certain architectural traits.
The study showed that the experienced designers interviewed found 
it paramount for the project that all stakeholders take a integrat­
ed holistic approach if decent architecture, good collaboration and 
well-performing buildings are to be achieved when applying DGNB.

VII
Preface
Writing this thesis has deepened my reflections on why I chose ar­
chitectural engineering with a master thesis in sustainable building 
assessments. Throughout the years, ever since public school, have I 
been made aware of the global environmental issue that has become 
ever more critical since my first understanding of what can be de­
fined as the most difficult task for the human species so far. Choosing 
architectural engineering, due to my interest of engineering and the 
built environment, made me understand the scope of the problem 
and provoked my ambition for deeper understanding. This, at first, 
drove me to study the essential physics of heat and mass balances of 
a building, along with its indoor climate, and how to make these are­
as efficient through in a classic engineering approach. This resulted 
in my Bachelor thesis where aspects like energy, daylight, moisture 
and fungus were applied in architectural competitions. This directed 
me towards a more comprehensive assessment on my post-graduate 
education and has ultimately led me to the area of sustainable build­
ing assessments with the a naive hope of creating better buildings 
that is embraced by the future. I hope my work done within this the­
sis, which ended up with a quite different academic approach than 
first thought, will be a tiny piece of knowledge that can contribute to 
the creation of buildings that truly embraces the world in the three 
dimensions of sustainability. 
First of all, I would like to thank my supervisor Lotte Bjerregaard 
Jensen, for creating a benign frame for this project and assisting 
and guiding the me towards the answers of the research objective. 
I would also like to thank the experts Ida Garre, Jan Kauschen, 
Jørn Kiesslinger and Camilla Dyring that volunteered to share their 
knowledge about this topic with me - it became the very essence 
and backbone of the entire project and it would not have been pos­
sible without these contributions. Furthermore, I wish to thank my 
fellow students that, throughout the years of university, created an 
inspiring environment and being an essential and excellent sounding 
board for the understanding and cultivation of knowledge.
Finally, I wish to thank my family and my friends for maintaining an 
interest in my studies and supporting me in the time of need.
Kasper Espenhein
Copenhagen, September 22nd, 2016.

INTRODUCTION
Part I
This part will introduce the reader to the dilemma and general area 
of research. It will guide the reader to the essence of the research 
question that is sought to be answered in this master thesis. The part 
will also inform the reader about the structure of the thesis and how 
it seeks to guide the reader through the document.

The dilemma
3
1	 The dilemma
While writing the introduction to this thesis has the leading scien­
tists yet again measured the warmest month of June ever record­
ed since the start of world average temperature measuring in 1850. 
This follows just after a record breaking seven month of new hottest 
months (Slezak, 2016), which are one of many incremental steps in 
the global warming. This can be seen in NASA’s measurements that 
states a temperature increase of 1.21 in world average since pre-in­
dustrial age, along side with great variations in the different climate 
zones world wide (GISTEMP Team, 2016; Hansen et al., 2010) The 
temperature increase can be seen in Figure 1 and Figure 2. The cur­
rent  temperature increase defines by NASA is 0.39 degree celsius 
from the limit of 1.5 that was adopted at COP 21 in Paris (UNFCCC, 
2015). 
The global warming is, however, not the only challenge that the 
world is facing. It is one of the most urgent aspect to take actions 
upon, but other issues need attention as well, and they will need to 
be addressed soon if not to become equally critical. This is visible 
when the view is broadened so areas like, material usage, bio-diversi­
ty, acidification are covered as well. The limit is, for example, for bi­
odiversty loss, in 2010, exceeded tenfold if one addresses the theory 
of “Planetary Boundaries” by RockStröm et al. (2009) which is illus­

Introduction
trated in Figure 3. The UN Convention for Biological Diversity also 
calls for actions against “business as usual” production, behaviour 
and consumption in order to avoid substantial loss of biodiversity 
(Secretariat of the Convention on Biological Diversity, 2014). How­
ever, the view can be broadened yet again, resulting in even other 
issues appearing like the world economical situation or the world 
social wealth difference. The ecological consequences that the world 
are facing at the moment are by far the most critical to address, but 
the economic and social elements should neither be neglected. 
These issues has, to some extent, their roots in buildings and the 
built environment. A common fact is that the energy consumption 
of the world building mass accounts for over 40% of the total energy 
consumption. The building sector is at the same time a big part of 
the world economy. It is alone related to 10-40% of countries GPD 
and accounts for about 10% of all jobs in the world (UNEP SBCI, 
2013). This has furthermore to be seen in relation to us spending the 
majority of time inside our houses and that 70% of the world pop­
ulation will live in urban areas by 2050 (UN Habitat, 2009). This 
combined underlines the fact about buildings and the building sector 
being a fundamentally important element in the modern world that 
must relate to the impacts within all three aspects of sustainability. 
1.1	
The barrier
The dilemma of avoiding pushing the world into irreversible chang­
es places an increasing number of restrictions and performance re­
quirements on modern buildings which they must cope with, and this 
feeds the need for assessing buildings with a sustainable assessment 
more than ever before. Previous was the energy design in focus, and 
the complexity of obtaining a building with a low energy usage was 
difficult, but it was catalysed by the creation of the Integrated Energy 
Design (IED) assessment (Brunsgaard, 2016; IEA, 2003). Different 
versions of this approach appeared throughout the years after the 
invention of IED due to different desires and focus areas, among 
these are Integrated Design Process (IEA, 2003), Integrated Pro­
ject Delivery (AIA, 2007) and Integrated Energy Design (Gaarsted, 
Kamper and Høiberg, 2007). 
New approaches now emerging, despite these initiatives, due to 
awarenes of the bigger picture and the need of a broader approach 
than sorely energy assessments. These approaches are called Build­
ing Sustainability Assessment Tool (BSAT). A BSAT addresses a

==PAGE:17==
The dilemma
5
building holistically through the dimensions of sustainability and of­
fers a long list of criteria that has to be evaluated within the whole 
buildings life cycle and taken into an account when designing the 
building. This makes the aspect of sustainability assessments and 
certification a complex affair because of several different things like 
its application on a building and the dimensions of sustainability - 
but also due to the vast numbers of trade-offs that constantly have 
to be made during the design process (Schweber, 2013; Kreiner, 
Passer and Wallbaum, 2015). This challenge becomes even more 
pressing in the early design phase, where Ding (2008) argue that 
BSATs can have the highest possible influence due to the project 
being created and virtually no parameters are fixed, just as any other 
aspect in the assessment of a project with Integrated Energy Design 
(Löhnert, Dalkowski and Sutter, 2003). It is difficult in this stage for 
team members, such as architects, structural or MEP engineers to 
know which parameters that have a low or high impact on the total 
measured sustainability, but also when these parameters should be 
addressed. Questions regarding the general assessment might arise 
within the team where possible questions could be: Why is there 
placed so many restrictions to the architecture?, How much effort 
shall be put into the LCC calculations, Why do we need to save and 
create these calculation? or why should we lower work and expecta­
tions on the thermal indoor climate?
There might also be questions about how to work with these parame­
ters. Should they be dealt with early? Do they require much attention 
in the start? and how are the things supposed to be documented? 
The BSAT assessor that acts as a process manager tries to cultivate 
and catalyse this process, but instead tends to end up as the only one 
with a decent overview of the project. It might be that the assessor 
tries to communicate to specific things, but it can have limited effect 
if the design team is unaware of the assessors role and importance 
for the project. This places a significant of pressure and dependency 
on the assessor who, due to this, has a considerable impact on the fi­
nal outcome (Brunsgaard, 2016). The result is a somehow inefficient 
process that creates frustrations at team members because they are 
unaware of what is happening and why different new requirements 
appears all the time, and this is in many ways against an optimal 
situation for creating a decent design (Brunsgaard & Larsen, 2015). 
This problem is not unique, nor new, and shows the need for opti­
mising and making the job of working with BSAT easier and more 
tangible. Much research has also been done within this area since 
==PAGE:18==
6
Introduction
the first versions of BSATs, and this can generally be categorised 
into two assessments. The first assessment takes the aim of obtain­
ing a better understanding  of the BSATs. This leads to the analysis 
and mapping of BSAT’s structural setup and their criteria’s interre­
lations and importance. The second assessment takes the aim of un­
derstanding BSAT’s impact on the design process and things related 
to this process. This leads to the investigation of different cases and 
BSATs impact on e.g. the design process, the management, the client 
or the design team.
The two assessments are different but can still be argued to have an 
impact on each other while also informing the general understanding 
of a BSAT, especially now when the BSATs now starts to incorporate 
the design process as part of the sustainability score (DGNB, 2016). 
Yet no research were found where a researcher evaluated both as­
sessments in one study with the desire of creating more tangible and 
practical guidelines for design teams. This lack of research, where 
both assessment methods contribute to the understanding of BSATs 
impact on a building and its design process, has been argued to 
hold possible findings that would benefit the overall understanding 
(Schweber, 2013; Schweber and Leiringer, 2012). This fact reveals 
space for more research and leads to this thesis seeking to contrib­
ute within the framework of these ideas.
==PAGE:19==
The aim and objective
7
2	 The aim and objective
The dilemma presented before justify the need to understand and 
map the sustainability tools and processes used in relation to the 
creation of buildings in order to cultivate the final possible building 
design and its sustainability level. This creates the foundation for 
this thesis and leads to the following aim:
To investigate and map the structure of BSATs in order to 
find, understand and synthesise key aspects that together 
can catalyse the usage of BSATs in a design process.
But what does this include and what does it take to obtain the re­
quired knowledge to reach the aim? The aim must be translated into 
a clear objective since this will work as the baseline for the research 
questions that delivers the required knowledge. This leads to the 
following objective:
To obtain thorough insight of how BSATs work, how they 
are structured, how they are used by a team and how they 
are applied on a building in a design process.
Figure 4 illustrates the research objective a condensed version that 
can be argued to just adequately define the very core of the research 
objective. The full objective are still the very definition for this re­
search, however, the figure will create the foundation for a visual 
illustration of this thesis development throughout the document. 
The research objective offers many possible aspects to focus upon 
when searching for answers to fulfil it. One aspect can be argued 
to be the theoretical setup of the BSAT itself. The developers of a 
BSAT argue that they put sustainability on formula, and this means 
that a certain input will lead to a certain output. Hence, obtaining 
a thorough understanding of this theoretical formula based setup 
will increase the usability. A research question that would grant the 
necessary knowledge when answered would then be:
- Which criteria are important to address and how difficult are they to 
work with due to their complexity and interrelations to other criteria.
Aim
Objective
Research question - BSAT theory
Optimise
BSAT usage 
Figure 4 - The research objective 
core. Note that this figure will be 
expanded with knowledge present­
ed and described in this document.
==PAGE:20==
8
Introduction
Furthermore does the implementation of a BSAT have an impact 
on the project because it places specific requirements in almost 
every aspect of a project. These requirements can be to areas such 
as documentation, collaboration or calculations etc., and the result 
is a change in the workflow when compared to a project without a 
BSAT implemented because the specific requirements will have to 
be solved in a specific way. This leads to the workflow with BSATs 
becoming the second aspect with the following question:
- How does BSATs implement to the design process, and does the BSATs 
have criteria that should be addressed earlier than others during the 
design phases?
Lastly does a BSAT place a large number of requirements to the 
engineering solutions present in the building. The has an impact on 
both the engineering solutions, but also the architectural solution. 
Yet requirements to the engineering solutions is not a new thing 
since they are frequently changed by different stakeholders such as 
the authorities, but they also have impact on the architecture since 
the architecture must adapt and transform to fit these requirements. 
One might then ask what type of impact a BSAT has on the archi­
tecture. It cannot be completely random impacts since the formula 
based structure of BSATs withholds certain generic elements. This 
leads to the third and last aspect of the research objective, where the 
following question can be asked to obtain knowledge that will feed 
into the research objective:
- What happens with the architecture of a building when there is an 
aim of achieving a high score in BSATs .
In conclusion illustrates these three research questions the three as­
pects of the research objective, thus creating a tangible foundation for 
the future research. The three aspects can be seen in Figure 5, where 
they are put into relation with the core of the research objective.
2.1	 Delimitations 
The challenges with BSATs the design process are not an unique el­
ement for a our region of the world, but rather a universal challenge 
that will become increasingly present throughout the coming years. 
This means that the current scope of the research objective holds too 
many potential aspects that this research can investigate, and this 
calls for the creation of research delimitations. In order to obtain a 
Research question - Workflow
Research question - Architecture

The aim and objective
9
fitting delimitation must the delimitations lead to a healthy balance 
between the available time, the level of detail in the investigated as­
pects and the available empirical data accessible. 
In short leads the defined delimitations below to the summary de­
fined in Table 1. However, the following text will describe in detail 
why these delimitations has been chosen for creating a healthy frame 
for this research:
Amount of BSATs - The amount of BSATs throughout the world 
increases rapidly these days, and they all differ from each 
other due to different focus on the dimensions sustainability. 
Furthermore are they also different due to climatic, cultural 
and legal differences in the countries. Thus doing the research 
with numerous BSATs require that these aspect are taken into 
consideration as well. This will not happen in this research as 
it will have an impact of the possible level of detail, and it is for 
this reason chosen to only work with DGNB as a certification 
system. 
Location - The location plays a crucial role when considering the de­
sign process and the local adaptations in BSATs. This means 
that empirical data found in e.g. Germany cannot be directly 
used in the research, but must be considered with the different

Introduction
set of boundary conditions as stated above. Based on this, is it 
chosen to restrict the research to the Danish building sector. 
This restriction is, however, not valid for the literature study.
Certification scheme - The use of DGNB as BSAT still leaves a num­
ber of different certification systems each with their own focus. 
The Danish adaption of DGNB includes Office Buildings (OB), 
Multi Storey Housing (MSH), Educational and Children In­

additionally under currently adaptation to the Danish market. 
The choice of delimitation here has to focus only on Office 
buildings since it was the first certification system to be imple­
mented in Denmark, meaning that it should hold the biggest 
field of knowledge and experience in the Danish sector. 
2.2	 Methodology
The research objective can be met throught different methods, yet 
it is important to note that the some of the questions for the as­
pects are best answered throught the gathering of knowledge and 
experience from the Danish building sector. This narrows it down 
to the choice between case based data collection or exptert based 
data collection. Each option has its advantages/disadvantages, but 
evaluating the possibilities seen in the light of the narrow timeframe 
a master thesis holds can it be concluded that an exptert based data 
collection might be most suitable. This means that DGNB experts 
from the Danish building sector will act as empirical data sources to 
form the answer for the research objective.
However, this choise withholds many considerations in terms of 
what type of data to collect along with what type of analysis tool to 
use for the creation of a synthesis. These considerations are treated 
in a much larger detail in Part III - Methodology where a deduction 
of the most fitting analysis tool also happens. Furthermore explains 
Chapter 10 the methodology used within the thesis. This means that 
Part II - Background does not relate to any specific methodology 
except for the search term index used to scan the databases for liter­
ature relevant for the research objective. The search term index can 
also be found described in Chapter 10. 
==PAGE:23==
Thesis structure
11
3	 Thesis structure
This document has been divided based on inspiration from the IM­
RAD structure. Each part, defined the the Roman numerals, seeks 
out to scope the enteties that a normal IMRAD approach has. There 
are deviations however, which can be seen in the figure to the right. 
The Introduction has been divided into Part I and Part II because 
of the size. This means that Part I only scopes the research intro­
duction and the research objective, whereas Part II relates to the 
background literature relevant for the understanding og BSATs. Part 
II ends with an in conclusion that shortly summarises the key aspects 
found in the part. 
Part III scopes the methodology for this thesis, and this includes the 
work progress along with the tools used. However, since the meth­
odology is an important aspect for this thesis, are the theory for the 
tools used in the methodology explained as the first thing within this 
Part. The part also ends with an in conclusion that shortly summa­
rises the key aspects found. 
Part IV presents the Findings from the experts and structures them 
according to the three differet aspects of the research objective as 
seen on Figure 5. The part also ends with an in conclusion that 
shortly summarises the key aspects found.
Lastly in the IMRAD structure presents Part V the Analysis and Dis­
cussion of the results. The findings from Part IV is put into relation 
with the ressearch objective along with a comparison of the findings 
and the literature found in Part II. The part ends with the global 
conclusion for this research.
The last two parts, Part VI and Part VII is beyon the scope of the 
IMRAD structure, but Part VI holds the bibliography while Part VII 
holds all the appendices that was found relevant for transparency 
and justification for the work within this thesis.

BACKGROUND

==PAGE:27==
Sustainability
15
4	 Sustainability
The increasing popularity of the term Sustainability over the last 
decade has led it to become a modern buzzword that is used ex­
tensively by governments, corporations and organisations in every 
situation possible to define a modern and new approach. This has 
certainly something to do with the terms applicability because the 
aim is tangible. The issue is, however, that fulfilling the aim is an­
other story and this is often forgotten by the majority of the users. 
The extensive use has resulted in a blurry understanding of what it 
originally defines, and has casted a shadow over the sincerity of the 
word. This were already debated on a high scale in 2008, where 
a British think tank, named Centre for Policy Studies, released a 
report about Buzzwords and modern words. Here they noted that: 
“Few words have become more heavily used or abused in government 
and corporate affairs than ‘sustainable’“ and they continue with “its 
very ubiquity has robbed it of meaning, while corrupting the principle 
or activity to which it is attached” (Centre for Policy Studies, 2009). 
Due to this issue will the term and its relation to certification systems 
in buildings be addressed in the coming text where the origin, aim, 
usability and its relation to buildings will be described. 
4.1	 The origin of Sustainability
The mindset of sustainability has been around long before its first in­
ternational use in 1969 by IUCN, yet it was not called sustainability 
but existed in many other terms and relations. It first appeared in­
ternationally in 1969 in a mandate in IUCN, through the talk about 
“The perpetuation and enhancement of the living world” (Adams, 
2016). It then gained increasingly attention throughout the years 
and was progressively developed throughout several UN conferenc­
es. This resulted in the establishment of the World Commission on 
Environment and Development (WCED) in 1983 that released their 
report in 1987 called “Our Common Future”, also referred to as 
“The Brundtland report”. The sustainable development were here 
defined as:
Centre for Policy Studies is 
a British policy think tank 
founded in 1974 by Marga­
ret Thatcher, among others, 
that champion for economic 
liberalism.
IUCN is an abbreviation 
for: International Union for 
Conservation of Nature.
“Sustainable development is development that meets the needs of 
the present without compromising the ability of future generations to 
meet their own needs.”
- Brundtland Commission
“
==PAGE:28==
16
Background
(UN WCED, 1987). The concept was further developed over the 
years and in 1992 at the UN conference in Rio de Janeiro, it was 
written that the UN wanted sustainable development in dimensions 
as the social and economical as well as environmental (UNCED, 
1992). These dimensions were later translated to the three pillars of 
sustainability as seen in Figure 7, during the creation of The IUCN 
Programme 2005-2008 (IUCN, 2004). This became the general 
adopted version that we use to explain and assess sustainability in 
the current days. However, the same programme also advocated for 
another illustration, as seen to the right in Figure 7, because it “rec­
ognises the dependency of society and its related economic activity on 
environmental health” as the programme defines it. The same pro­
gramme also recognised that challenges existed for whether the cur­
rent approaches towards sustainable development actually achieved 
sustainability. This resulted in the programme defining a number 
key result areas that should act as guides to bring the world closer to 
a sustainable development. 
The sustainable development are, however, not easy to define and 
many has since the creation of the sustainable concept and its dimen­
sions has gone wrong of the difference between a sustainable assess­
ment, and something becoming and being sustainable. This can be 
explained by the incredible complexity there lies within the answer 
to the threshold for where something genuinely becomes sustainable.
4.2	 Threshold for sustainability
The thresholds for sustainability has tried to be determined since the 
understanding of the issue, but there is still today a debate about the 
Figure 7 - Different models and illustrations of the dimensions or pillars of sustain­
able development.

==PAGE:29==
Sustainability
17
certainty of the thresholds currently defined. Hence, one can con­
clude that a general world-wide agreement and understanding of the 
actual threshold is still missing, as illustrated in Figure 8. Yet there is 
one area that has come closer to the threshold than others, but this 
is also due to a big effort, and this dimension is the ecological dimen­
sion. This is because it has received a world-wide focus throughout 
a long period due to out awareness of our current pace of fossil fuel 
burning, material excavating etc. that has the consequence of un­
known irreversible changes. Yet it must also be mentioned that the 
ecological dimension still has a long way to go. Take global warming 
as an example of this. 
Global warming has been the most discussed and mentioned conse­
quence to date (Turner, 2008), and this has also led to it being an 
area that has received the biggest focus of defining where its thresh­
old for sustainability is. Different studies has also brought us closer 
to some of these thresholds. As example can the planetary bound­
aries be mentioned, which has proposed a biodiversity loss of 10 
species per million species per year, or a water quality threshold of 
50mg/l set for nitrogen in the ground water (RockStröm et al., 2009; 
Srebotnjak, 2010). The general world focus on the global warming 
can also be seen in the earliest versions of certifications systems for 
buildings. These were designed to assess buildings with the aim of 
minimising their energy usage and thus become an element pushing 
in the right direction of the sustainable threshold (Ebert, Eßig & 
Hauser, 2011). Yet, the environmental aspect must be accompanied 
the two other aspects to obtain the genuine sustainability threshold, 
but getting there is a difficult task.
In general, finding the thresholds within the economical, ecological 
or social dimension is a highly complex issue that is not yet solved. 
We must instead, while we wait, take the other approach and do a 
sustainable assessment with state-of-the-art knowledge in order to 
get as close to the unknown thresholds as possible. This mindset 
valid in many different things today, and it is especially also valid for 
the building sector. Especially when it is seen in the light of the dif­
ferent aims set for buildings by the organisations and governments 
throughout the world, and this call for the use of new Building Sus­
tainability Assessment Tools (BSATs) that can quantify the buildings 
sustainable impact.

5	 Quantified sustainability
The general aim of Building Sustainability Assessment Tools 
(BSATs)  is to quantify and evaluate the different impacts of a build­
ing in the three dimensions of sustainability to determine a certain 
performance level. This performance level indicates how well the 
constructed building’s impact relates to the social, economical and 
environmental dimensions. The performance level is evaluated on 
a certain amount of criteria within each dimension of sustainability, 
and they usually vary from 2 to 20 for each dimension. The criteria 
within the each dimension is weighted according to e.g. their impact, 
political importance. A specific example to this is explained more in 
detail in Section 5.1.
The first BSATs came in the start of 1990 with the aim of measuring 
and reducing buildings energy usage through a common framework 
platform that made the buildings comparable (Ebert, Eißig & Haus­
er, 2011). The Building Research Establishment Environmental As­
sessment Method (BREEAM) was the first comprehensive building 
assessment tool that was introduced in 1990 by the British Building 
Research Establishment (BRE) with the aim of developing cost ef­
fective and energy efficient assessments of buildings (BRE, 2016). 
It started out as a small assessment method with a few criteria and 
a single certification scheme for office buildings only, but then de­
veloped throughout the years with the scope of criteria becoming 
bigger and at the same time addressing a variety of buildings. It 
has nowadays become one of the biggest and most adapted BSATs 
throughout the world, with about 77 country-adaptations and over 
half a million certifications to date (BRE, 2016). The Leadership in 
Energy and Environmental Design (LEED) was introduced in 1994 
in the USA with the aim to measure “green buildings”. LEED was 
created by US Green Building Council, which is a non-profit organ­
isation, whereas BREEAM was created by a governmental depart­
ment that later in 1997 became privatised. The scope and focus of 
these assessment tools had, at that time, minor differences but were 
generally addressing the same focus of reducing of the energy usage. 
This categorises them as tools in the first generation of the BSATs 
according to Ebert, Eißig & Hauser (2011) as illustrated in Figure 9. 
The French construction organisations developed at the same time 
their own version of a similar tool which they called Haute Qualité

Envrionmentale (HQE), but this deviates slightly from the other big­
ger systems due to its focus on achieving the goals for sustainability 
mainly through the design process.
Since the first versions of BREEAM, HQE and LEED has new certi­
fication systems, or BSATS, been introduced. This is a result of the 
increasing level of awareness from the world, but also because of the 
systems increasing level of detail that makes differences in climat­
ic, cultural and legal boundaries important, and this has ultimately 
made them very specific in their geographical locations. They also 
differ in approach of how to measure, the scope of the sustainable 
dimension, the method in how to calculate, and many other parame­
ters that makes them unique in an equal field (Ebert, Eißig & Hauser, 
2011). Figure 10 shows some of the larger certification systems and 
their belonging origin and Figure 9 shows the development of the 
different BSATs throughout the time.
Some countries, like Canada, Brazil, Netherlands and Denmark, has 
not developed their own certification system but instead implement­
ed and modified a system from a similar country that they found 
most suitable for the country market. DGNB was chosen by Green 
Building Council Denmark to be the certification system used on 
the Danish market after a comprehensive case based study with 
BREEAM, DGNB, HQE and LEED and their advantages/disad­
vantages for the Danish market (Birgitsdottir et al., 2010). The aim 
of the first BSAT generation were, as written earlier in relation to

BREEAM, to optimise and work with energy efficiency. They devel­
oped along with the society’s  understanding of the limited resources 
and came to have bigger and bigger scope and importance. However, 
they did not relate completely to the three pillars of sustainability. 
This changed with the second generation is BSATs that incorporat­
ed both economy and social criteria in the assessmet. The second 
generation BSATs can also be seen in Figure 9 and includes, among 
others, the European Unions project called Label for Environmental, 
Social and Economic Buildings (LEnSE) that later has evolved into 
OPEN HOUSE (BBRI, 2006; CCS, 2010), the SBTool by the iiSBE 
organisation (iiSBE, 2009) and DGNB (DGNB, 2016). 
5.1	 DGNB
DGNB was developed in 2007 by the German Sustainable Building 
Council due to the increasing demand for assessing buildings in a 
sustainable approach in Germany, and also due to the amount of 
foreign BSATs that had taken a hold in German building sector. It is 
primarily based on European standards and framework from previ­
ous tools like LEnSE and SBtool, but it has also been based on ex­
perience and knowledge from the previous systems like LEED and 
BREEAM. This makes the framework relatively similar between the 
majority of the European countries. It is, as mentioned earlier, de­
fined as a second generation tool because it scopes all three aspects, 
but it does it to a much higher degree than other second generation 
BSATs (Khezri, 2011; Andrade and Bragança, 2016). This can also 
be seen in the three general objectives of DGNB, which are: 1. Pro­
tection of environment and natural resources, 2. Reduction in life 
cycle costs and guarantee economic value and 3. Assurance of health 
and guarantee of social and cultural values (DGNB, 2016; Ebert, 
Eißig & Hauser, 2011). Furthermore does it also relate to other as­
pect of a building such as Technical quality as well as Process quality. 
The Danish version of DGNB defines its six different areas, which is 
shown in Figure 11, the following way:
Environmental quality - DGNB evaluates the environmental im­
pact based on the following seven indicators, which are in ac­
cordance with the local standards (Danish Standards, 2012): 
Global warming potential, Ozone depletion potential, photo­
chemical ozone creation potential, Acidification potential, Eu­
trophication potential. Furthermore evaluates DGNB also the 
building on its embodied energy, where the total primary ener­
gy, non-renewable energy and renewable energy are evaluated. 
This is done over a period of 50 years with the use of different

==PAGE:34==

Background
European standards and databases together with the Environ­
mental Product Declaration (EPD) of every material used.
Economic quality - This aspect incorporates the aspects such as Life 
Cycle Costs (LCC) with the aim of reducing the total costs for 
the building during its life time. The running costs are, how­
ever, especially in focus here. The calculations are performed 
with a tool called LCCbyg in accordance with the current ISO 
standards (Danish Building Research Institute, 2016; ISO, 
2008).
Social quality - This area focuses predominantly on health and user 
satisfaction. It separates out the different aspects of comfort 
and also scopes the buildings functionality and partly also the 
architectural quality of the building.
Technical quality - DGNB focuses also on the technical quality of 
the classic building aspects such as the technical quality of 
the solutions. More specifically does this scope areas such as 
sound insulation, building envelope, future adaptability and 
disassembly. 
Process quality - This scopes the quality of the planning and the 
design process of the building along with the construction pro­
cess. It seeks to create guidelines and recommendations for 
the management of the process to integrate DGNB in a seam­
less way to the design process of a building. 
Site quality - The site aspect is evaluated separately from the building 
and has no effect on the final points given. Here the buildings 
location and relation to its surroundings is considered where 
aspects like transport access and local urban life is included.

Background
Together, all these six categories make up the foundation for the eval­
uation as illustrated in Figure 11. A detailed list of each of the six 
main categories can be found in Table 2, and each criterion’s size is 
also show separated in their main categories in Figure 12. 
The certification process with DGNB is voluntary and can be han­
dled either by a DGNB consultant or by a DGNB auditor. Further­
more does a client have the possibility to do a pre-certification dur­
ing the design process. The intention of the pre-certification is to 
set and determine sustainability objectives during the design phase, 
which can bring clarity to the project and its level of ambitiousness. 
When the building is completed the final certification process can 
take place. This process must be done by a DGNB auditor who is 
responsible for gathering and handling all the relevant documenta­
tion. The auditor is furthermore responsible for other administrative 
work such as registering the project at DGNB etc. A building must 
as a last step, when the building has been build and all the documen­
tation has been created and gathered, undergo a conformity check. 
This is  where a third party of experts check and evaluate the docu­
mentation for the building, and the building will obtain its certificate 
if the conformity check finds no lack of documentation. 
The certificate award can be either bronze, silver, gold or platinum. 
The certificate level vary according to the buildings total perfor­
mance. The total performance is determined by weighting and sum­
mating  the scores of the 40 individual criteria. The performance 
within each of the main categories, such as environment or process, 
must not be too low since there exist a lower limit that each of the 
five main categories must comply with, if a certain score is desired. 
This is to ensure that a truly holistic approach is kept and that dodgy 
performance in parts of the main categories is avoided. The spe­
cific certification levels and the corresponding total and minimum 
performance indexes can be found in Table 3. DGNB states that 
close to 80% of the required documentation has to be done anyway 
compared to a conventional design project. However, the formal re­
quired from DGNB has not been considered in this statement (Ebert, 
Eißig & Hauser, 2011). Note that this statement is only for the Ger­
man version and that it might not be valid in other countries. 
In 2015 had the German DGNB 12 different certification schemes 
where the Urban District included four different types of districts, 
and this scopes from new and existing office buildings, healthcare, 
dwellings etc. The Danish variant had in 2016 five different schemes

hospitals. The non-German variants has undergone changes to the 
boundary conditions so the local rules, standards, practices and cul­
ture are implemented to fit the given country in an adequate way. 
This means that the country specific certifications differ in many of 
the minor things, whereas the general core or framework for the 
BSAT is identical in all country versions. 
Variations in the different schemes of DGNB and its relation to other 
BSATS like BREEAM and LEED are another big and relevant area 
that is relevant to understand since it contributes to the total under­
standing of the worldwide approach to sustainable certification. The 
following section will make a comparison of DGNB with the two 
other major BSATs, BREEAM and LEED.
5.2	 Comparison
Many studies has been done on BSATs where the researchers evalu­
ate different areas like scope, structure, certification setup. This has 
been done both internally in a BSAT, like LEED Homes and LEED 
New Constructions, but also externally between the different BSATs 
like LEED and BREEAM or HQE and DGNB. Both Wallhagen et al. 
(2013), Andrade and Bragança (2016), Ding (2008), Eberl (2010), 
Eberl and Essig (2013), Khezri (2011) and Wangel et al. (2016) all 
concluded there exists considerable differences, in almost all aspects

figures. Figure 14 is a study from Khezri (2011) and it shows the dif­

for other schemes, e.g. dwellings, is also the same (Andrade and Bra­

and Bragança (2016) all concludes that DGNB is the only BSAT the 
truly covers economical aspects, whereas other only partly cover this 
aspect. This can also be seen from Figure 14, where DGNB shows a 
more equal focus on its main categories, and contrary focuses LEED 
mostly on the environmental elements. This variation has made 
some of the authors declare some areas within BSATs for subjective­
ly assessments, and this is also when taking in local uniqueness into 
consideration. The authors continiue the bold statement by advocat­
ing for more international standards on the area. Many of the studies 
mentioned also finds that the newest BSATs have a tendency to be

Wallhagen et al. (2013) divides up the different sustainability sys­
tem’s criteria into three different categories. These are specified 
according to the scope, detail and action they are to cover. The cat­
egories are Procedural, Feature and Performance. Wallhagen et al. 
(2013) defines them as:
Procedural - This cover the criteria that involves processes, assess­
ments, activities etc. Their intention is to credit actions taken 
toward the improvement of the level of sustainability. Exam­
ples of this are integrated design or commissioning.
Feature - This scopes means or measures that is assessed with a 
specific technical solution. An example could be individual­
ly controlled climate zones or green roofs. These means ore 
measures are typically easy to determine if they fulfill the re­
quirement or not.
Performance - This cover criteria that are complex in their setup and 
require comprehensive calculations to obtain a result. Exam­
ples are yearly energy usage or global warming potential.
The findings show that every BSAT evaluated according to this as­
sessment has a variety of each of the three defininitions. The authors 
furthermore find that each category works best for specific parts of a 
design process. Especially is the Procedural evaluated as a possible 
tool to improve the likelihood of obtaining a better result through

Architecture is barely mentioned in all the specified literature. Only 
two articles addresses the architectural aspects. Khezri (2011) con­
cludes that the use of BSATs lead to energy savings in green build­
ings but more work needs to be done with BSATs to ensure a high­
er success with bio-climatic architecture. Yuce (2012) evaluates 
the importance of the generic categories from DGNB, LEED and 
BREEAM, which is seen in Table 6. They were evaluated accord­
ing to a criteria-ranking where the different criteria were ranked ac­
cording to their importance. The criteria ranking were created in a 
project under the European Commission called SuPerBuildings (Su­
PerBuildings, 2012). The findings defines and categorises architec­
ture and cultural considerations as less important compared to com­
fort, energy and materials, which are categorised as very important. 
The whole categorisation can be found in Table 6. This assertion of 
architecture and culture being less important is also addressed by 
Naboni (2013) who argues that architectural values lacks presence 
in all three mentioned certification systems, i.e. BREEAM, DGNB 
and LEED.

BSAT optimisation
29
6	 BSAT optimisation
Many has studied the possibility of optimising the usability of BSATs 
and thus also the design process of working with them because it is 
a highly complex and comprehensive task. Studies has taken place in 
different fields of the BSAT environments that scopes, among others, 
Design process optimisation and Multi-Criteria Decision Making 
(MCDM).
A lot of literature has been found on MCDM along with the manage­
ment and process optimisation of BSATs. Yet less has been found on 
the assessment of sustainability systems to make it a useful design 
tool in the early design phases, and even less on the optimisation 
by the use of BIM. The following sections will describe the litera­
ture found on BSAT-optimisation and divide it according to different 
types of assessment.
6.1	 BSATs and design process
Bruunsgaard and Larsen (2015) and Bruunsgaard (2016) alone per­
formed case studies on different hospital projects in Denmark to 
decode which effect the DGNB system has on the design team and 
the design process. It was found important to collaborate from the 
very beginning with the whole team along with the DGNB assessor. 
It was also especially important to get DGNB implemented early in 
a the design process to make it valuable for the project, since it went 
from being a evaluation tool to a design tool. The authors also found 
that the contract from has a great impact on the design process, but 
they also stated that a team’s commitment depended on feeling of 
ownership for the project. ths is equal to some findings of Schweber 
and Haroglu (2014) that did a similar study on BREEAM. In general 
found Bruunsgaard and Larsen (2015) that a BSAT-assessment “just 
added another layer to the project” along with another stakeholder. 
The similar study by Schweber and Haroglu (2014) explored and 
mapped the assessment with BREEAM on the design and construc­
tion process. The findings, besides the one mentioned, were that 
individual commitment from experts also had a great impact on the 
team’s collaboration and thus the final result. Additionally findings 
showed that the integration of BREEAM happened on three differ­
ent levels: thight-fit, punctual-fit and bolt-on-fit, where a clear connec­
tion between the result and type of fit were found. The three types

of fit is explained in Table 7. They also found that a better design 
process were present when all team members knew and understod 
the principles of the design process. Lastly their study also showed 
that an internal BREEAM assessor in many cases contributed notice­
ably to the design process, and that an assessor could compensate 
for other things lacking in the design team. Schweber (2013) studied 
alone the same cases but with another focus. Here the author’s fo­
cus were on the effects of BREEAM on the client, the team and the 
design process when it was added to the design process. The study 
investigated the effect in different cases where the teams knowledge 
about BREEAM varied. It was found that BREEAM had a consider­
able impact on the standard practices because of the bureaucratic 
method with documentation handling. It was concluded that it had 
the potential to undermine the client’s confidence on the tool. It was 
furthermore found that the system setup of BREEAM could lead to 
experts debating or rejecting the tool due to dissimilarities between 
the tool’s and the expert’s perception of sustainability. 
Takano and Kuittinen (2013) looked at the environmental impact 
of a building when the Life Cycle Inventory (LCI) differs in the level 
of detail. Three versions of the LCI that corresponded to different 
phases within a building project were analysed. It showed that a 
preliminary design level showed 50-65% of the values of a complete 
building, whereas the detailed design calculation showed 90-95% of 
the value. The authors stated that the findings could be used during 
the early design phases but at the same time defined that the relevant 
standards should address this issue to a higher degree. Novak (2013) 
studied the usability of Target Value Design (TVD) in the managing 
of delivering projects with a sustainable assessment. The motivation 
were that the TVD approach is suitable for ‘wicked problems’ and 
that sustainability in its nature can be defined as such. The study was 
done through different case studies. It was found that a team must 
set explicit values on sustainability to manage with TVD, but setting 
explicit goals also unified the teams visions for the project. Further­
more was it observed that TVD shifted the design focus from pure 
cost to a discussion of worth. It was concluded that TVD led to in­
cremental improvements in management, construction predictability 
and reducing costs in the design process. 
Lastly investigates Said et al. (2014) the concept of LEED’s eco-char­
rette with the aim of creating algorithms that can simulate stakehold­
ers behaviour when defining a client’s sustainability goals to increase 
the understanding of this area. The authors find that the computer

6.2	 Decision making in BSATs
There has been many attempts on making a BSAT more tangible, un­
derstandable and usable by using computational optimisation. Evins 
(2013) identified 74 studies that in different ways applied different 
types of optimisation methods on different fields of a building, how­
ever, only about half of them took a multi-criteria approach. Espe­
cially areas such as envelope design, building management systems 
(BMS), HVAC-systems and renewables were in focus. The main fo­
cus of these studies were to optimise energy and cost savings. The 
author concludes that the use of MCDM receives an increasing inter­
est because of the building sector’s gaining interest because the area 
holds many  great potentials.
Kreiner, Passer and Wallbaum (2015) analysed the Austrian adap­
tation of DGNB called ÖGNI for the possibility to create a system­
atic approach to the sustainability that could be utilised in the early 
design phase. This study was performed through the mapping of 
the criteria interdependency by using the MCDM method and the 
use of a Vester’s sensitivity model. Their findings clearly showed the 
internal structure of ÖGNI and each criteria’s importance. Figure 
15 shows some of the findings of the mapping. Each criterion has a 
active and passive influence, and a criterion with a large active influ­

Background
ence and a low passive influence are good for steering the project. 
A criterion with both a high active and passive influence are impor­
tant and has to be managed due to their interrelations with other 
criteria. Lastly the authors conclude that the work presented has the 
possibility of being implemented in BIM to improve the sustainable 
performance. Medineckiene et al. (2015) did a similar study with 
the Swedish version of sustainability certification system called Mil­
jöbyggnad together with a different MCDM method where an Ana­
lytic Hierarchic Process (AHP) method were used. It is concluded 
that the final output of using the MCDM guides the authors closer to 
an understanding of the setup Swedish system’s setup, impacts and 
usability when it had to be used in a design process. 
Madanayake and Ruwanpura (2012) did a similar study on construc­
tion optimisation with LEED and MCDCM where they developed 
a program to perform calculations instantly when new changes 
emerged to steer the project better. The authors expected the pro­
gram to help reducing time spent on defining LEED credits and iden­
tify criteria that has the most negative impact on the BSAT score. 
The findings should ultimately feed into a faster design approach. 
Doczy (2014) did a similar thing by developing a tool that imple­
mented in BIM and MCDM with the aim to analyse cost, LEED cred­
its and carbon neutrality faster. Yet another similar study performed 
by Choi (2015) created a tool that analysed the same system setup 
with LEED with the aim of easing the decision making in LEED’s 
eco-charrette. The authors of this study also came to the same con­
clusion that a program with these things implemented could lead to 
several optimisations.
Lastly evaluated Wong and Zhou (2015) about 84 studies performed 
on the integration of BIM to a buildings complete life cycle with the 
aim of reducing, reusing an recycling more. They concluded that 
many of the studies call for BIM to be implemented in the build­
ing and its entire life cycle. They also concludes that cloud-tools are 
needed to perform good management. 
==PAGE:45==
BSAT optimisation
33
in conclusion
The measure of sustainability is a complex affair that still lacks clar­
ity along with an actual threshold for the different dimension. This 
area is still unclear and can be argued today. Building Sustainability 
Assessment Tools, or BSATs, takes the approach of pushing towards 
the unknown threshold with their assessment of buildings in all 
three dimensions of sustainability. This makes them an even more 
complicated affair because they also needs to relate to the build en­
vironment. The result is vast list of criteria that weighs differently in 
the total evaluation and that, together with their evaluation of equal 
building components, creates a highly complex net of interconnec­
tions that makes designing a comprehensive and difficult task. This 
has led to many attempts on trying to make BSATs tangible and in­
crease the level of understanding and usability. Studies show that 
the design process can be catalysed by making a healthy contract 
that makes people work together from the very start, but also each 
team member’s commitment, mindset and feeling of ownership is 
of high importance. Mathematics has also shown to be helpful to 
understand the complex systems, and its integration with BIM-tools 
also catalyses the usage of the complex tool.

Part III
This part firstly contains a deduction of the correct research par­
adigm and belonging theories to use in data collection for the re­
search objective. It afterwards describes the relevant literature used 
in the methodology and finally explains the used methodology for 
the work within this thesis.
==PAGE:48==
Yes is more.
Bjarke Ingels
“
==PAGE:49==
Paradigms and theories
37
7	 Paradigms and theories
Defining which type of methodology and research paradigm to use 
in the approach to answer the research objective can be a challenge 
when working with topics that has the potential to give good results 
from both the interpretive and the positivist approach. The question 
is then to find out, which approach and belonging methodology that 
gives the most satisfactory result to the research objective. This sit­
uation is present in this thesis and the two different approaches will 
in the coming text be evaluated by their advantages/disadvantages 
in relation to fulfilling the research objective. Furthermore will the 
most suitable methodology in the chosen approach be defined along 
with a justification of why it has been chosen. This justification is 
based on the possibility of obtaining sound, reliable and valid results.
The first thing to consider are the fundamental differences and state­
ments of each approach. The positivist paradigm, sometimes also 
defines as the quantitative approach, devotes to the creation of the­
oretical propositions by the use of formal logic rules and hypothet­
ico-deductive rules, which is tested against the four requirements 
of theoretical propositions (Lee, 1991). This implies, among other 
things, that no subjective statements or non-proportioned parameters 
are acknowledged in the use of the theory. Lastly is this approach is 
predominantly used within the field of natural science,  such as civil 
engineering, physics etc. The interpretive paradigm, sometimes also 
defines as the qualitative approach, states that people develop their 
own meaning to the world around them and also to their own acting 
behaviour within it. These meanings are largely individual and there­
fore different from others, however, all are equally valid (Lee, 1991). 
This implies that the idea of one true solution is not acknowledged, 
and in order to understand another person’s point of view must one 
understand the differences in the way the other person observes the 
world and ultimately acts within it as well. This approach is predom­
inantly used in the field of social research.
Relating this to the area investigated could one easily argue for the 
positivist approach because DGNB has been built on elements from 
this paradigm. However, contradictions emerge when the three as­
pects of the research objective, as seen in Figure 16, is taken into 
consideration. The world of architecture, and all its aspects, rests 
The four requirements of the­
oretical propositions are:
Falsifiability, Logical consist­
ency, Relative explanatory 
power and Survival. See Lee 
(1991) for a more thorough 
explanation.

38
Methodology
highly within the interpretive field - it cannot be put into equation 
or completely quantified, even though many has tried but only part­
ly succeeded in their attempts. Furthermore is the workflow aspect 
also a field where the interpretive approach is predominantly used. 
This is because a construction project can be argued to be defined as 
a ‘wicked problem’ because it, to some degree, fulfil the definitions 
of a wicked problem by having: incomplete or contradictory knowl­
edge, a large number of opinions and people involved, a relatively 
large economic impact, and lastly the interconnected nature of these 
problems with other problems is high (Rittel and Webber, 1973). 
Thus Rittel and Webber (1973) states that a search for solutions 
through a positivist approach are bound to fail, and that we can only 
improve the situation but never solve it, no matter the effort.
Another thing to consider is the practical approach of each paradigm.
The positivist approach favour investigations based on quantity like 
calculations, quantity surveys, statistics or similar. Contrary favour 
the interpretive approach investigations that is based on viewpoints 
and understandings from a variety of people, and this often leads to 
interviews being the best option. Taking a look at some of the specif­
ic advantages and disadvantages, we see that there are the following 
things:
Interpretive approach
Advantage - In depth understanding of every argument and reason­
ing. Possibility of finding other aspects to the research objec­
tive that contributes to a better result.
Disadvantage - Time consuming. Little amount of participants. Less 
clear picture of differences and ranking.
Positivist approach
Advantage - Easy setup due to the hypothesis. Highly measurable 
differences and ranking of criteria. Possibility of measuring 
statistical parameters.
Disadvantage - Many people has to participate. It is difficult to know 
their underlying argument of their choices. Questionnaires 
may only partly be filled out. The data only shows results with­
in the given area of research.  
Summarising this leads to a conclusion where the interpretive ap­
proach is favoured by the workflow and the architecture aspect of 
the research objective. However, the approach to the theory aspect 
seem to be far from ideal. Yet it must be mentioned that addressing

of architecture.

the theory aspect through another paradigm is not wrong. In fact 
argues Schweber and Leiringer (2012) that the application of the 
interpretive research paradigm on the technical dimensions of the 
construction sector would enrich the area with new knowledge on 
many aspects. This could for example be tacit knowledge about the 
different interrelations between the criteria or it could be even other 
areas that has yet to be discovered.
It is clear to see, when evaluating the argument of Schweber and 
Leiringer (2012) to the presented literature in the previous part, that 
a sharp line divides the literature into the two research paradigms, 
with no exception of interference, hence leading to the conclusion 
that little literature, if not none, has used the interpretive approach 
on the setup of DGNB or any other BSAT. This division is illustrat­
ed in Table 8. Therefore, based on this reasoning, is the interpre­
tive approach found as the best and most interesting approach for 
answering the research objective and its three aspects, because it 
addresses the theory aspect in a new way, while at the same time 
gathering knowledge in the workflow and architecture aspect. This 
furthermore implies that interviews will be the best way of gathering 
knowledge for answering the research objective.
In the paradigm of interpretative research does several different 
research approaches exist, and each of them has been designed to 
assess a special kind of research field. Guest, Namey and Mitchell 
(2013) lists different approaches that are the most typical to use in 
the interpretive paradigm and defines them with their advantages 
and disadvantages according to the type of case they are to be ap­
plied upon. The details and usability of each of the approaches is 
beyond the scope of this thesis, yet the approach called Grounded 
Theory (GT) stands out as the most suitable and usable for the aim 
of this thesis since it used to build thematic structures for a theory,

==PAGE:53==
Grounded Theory
41
8	 Grounded Theory
Grounded Theory (GT) is an inductive, systematic and dynamic way 
of creating an understanding of the collected data, step by step, with 
the aim of generating a theory that explains the events, tendencies 
and structures within the data and the area of research (Charmaz, 
2006). GT is not a theory it self, but rather a method to develop 
a theory which is based on the collected data. The analysed data 
can be anything from notes, books, experiments or interviews. The 
process of GT is to gradually index, or code as the theory defines it, 
the research data and place it in categories so these categories can 
be evaluated individually, or in relation to other categories, in order 
to find tendencies and patterns that creates the foundation of the 
evolving theory.
A core element in the theory is the Constant Comparative Analysis, 
which is shown in Figure 18. It is a process that a researcher un­
knowingly follows because the researcher constantly has to re-eval­
uate the data, the codes, the categories and the patterns found and 
created dyring the work with the raw data. The result is that the 
researcher constantly finds new aspects emerging from the data be­

Methodology
cause his or her’s understanding of the knowledge has increased 
since the given data has been evaluated last time. This leads to yet 
other interpretations of the data, giving an even more nuanced pic­
ture of the research data analysed so far. The result is a iterative 
process that  continue until a point of saturation, which is where the 
researcher will stop finding other aspects in the text. The point of 
saturation is unknown for the researcher until a certain time in the 
process where it suddenly emerges.
GT was developed by two sociologists Barney Glaser and Anselm 
Strauss through their work with terminal hospital patients, which 
led them to write to write their first book about GT called “The 
Discovery of Grounded Theory” (Glaser and Strauss, 1967). The 
authors later obtained an individual and disparate understanding of 
their developed theory, and this led to the development of two dif­
ferent understandings of GT, each created by the individual author. 
Other scientists has nowadays proposed other versions of the theory, 
and this has resulted in different paradigms within GT that are cat­
egorised as the classic, the Constructionistic, the Straussian and the 
Feministic (Levers, 2013). Some of the paradigms focuses mainly at 
the anthropological essence of the theory, whereas other has taken 
a more practical approach. This thesis uses the definition and the 
methodology presented in the book written by Charmaz (2006) that 
works under the constructionistic paradigm. It is chosen because it 
offers a practical view on the theory along with a step by step defi­
nition of the theoretical frame that a researcher can follow. The GT 
process by Charmaz (2006) can be seen in Figure 19 and will be 
explained in detail in the coming sections.
8.1	 GT structure and process
GT is, as mentioned earlier, a list of assessments that is followed in 
order to create the a theory grounded in the research data. Each 
paradigm mentioned above has different definitions of the processes 
and the assessments. This makes the general process/methodology 
difficult to understand when studying different sets of literature, since 
the different steps of coding can be called several different things. 
The process presented by  Charmaz (2006), which is seen in Figure 
19, consists of five steps, each with different sub steps that one must 
follow in order to obtain a thorough theory. The different steps are: 
Data collection, Coding, Memoing, Sampling and Generation. 
The result of following each step is that the research data will be

Grounded Theory
43
condensed and categorised into smaller text pieces representing the 
knowledge found, which is then reassessed later in each of the fol­
lowing steps with the aim of achieving a more general and generic 
understanding in each of the steps. The result should be a theory that 
is based on the whole data set with argumentation that is strongly 
founded throughout the entire levels of conceptualisation, i.e. from 
raw data to conceptual synthesis of the theory.
8.2	 Initial Coding 
This is the first stage of the GT method. The aim of Initial Coding 
is to capture the core information in the data such as actions, state­
ments, points of view etc. in an objective way. It is here where the re­
searcher has the possibility to condense any information given in the 
research data to make it more tangible for the next phase. The initial 
coding can be done in different ways. These are: word-by-word cod­
ing, line-by-line coding and incident by incident coding. The suitabili­
ty of each ways differs according to the density of information within 
the given text. For interviews are line-by-line or incident-by-incident 
coding reccommemded, dependent on the complexity of the talk 
and area evaluated. Initial coding also has to deal with in vivo codes. 
These are defined as words, phrases or other that a certain group of 
people use to describe a given situation or thing, e.g. “old-fashioned 
guy” or “every cloud has a silver lining”.
8.3	 Focused Coding
Focused coding starts where initial coding ends by distilling and 
conceptualising the initial codes. Charmaz (2006) defines focused 
coding as: 
Thus a major activity in focused coding is finding connections and 
relations between the initial codes. In this relation, the focused 
codes becomes more active in their way of describing and defining, 
compared to the initial codes because they have to synthesise and 
describe generic tendencies. The focused codes can also scope sev­
eral initial codes, if the initial codes relate to the same thing. The fo­
Focused coding means using the most significant and/or frequent 
codes to sift through large amount of data. Focused coding requires 
decisions about which initial coding makes the most analytical sense 
to categorize your data incisively and completely.
- Kathy Charmaz
“
Strauss and Corbin (2007) 
calls this first step Open 
Coding, but the general 
assessment is very similar, if 
not identical.
==PAGE:56==
44
Methodology
cused codes must however still remain objective to the initial codes 
and stick to the essence of what it describes. This is because future 
coding rely on these findings, and a deviation here will lead to inva­
lid findings in the end. Charmaz (2006) states that another type of 
coding, developed by Strauss and Corbin (2007), called axial coding 
also can be a useful tool to do focused coding. She explains this with 
axial coding being the same as focused coding but it utilises visual 
illustrations like figures, mindmap or similar to a higher degree than 
focused coding. 
8.4	 Theoretical Coding
The theoretical codes starts to conceptualise the relations that the 
initial and focused codes might have. The conceptual codes are part­
ly created through the memo writing, which is described in the next 
section, and are the basically the researcher’s thoughts about inter­
relations or other aspects that exists within the data. The aim of 
the theoretical coding is to construct a frame or pattern of how the 
initial and focused codes relates to each other. One could argue that 
the initial and focused coding deconstruct the research data, and the 
aim for the theoretical codes are to relocate the frame/pattern. The 
definition from Glaser (1978) for theoretical codes are also similar, 
since he states that theoretical codes: 
Charmaz (2006) and Glaser and Strauss (1967) emphasize that the 
theoretical codes and the frame must appear from the text it self and 
not come from an outside. This is due to the risk of forcing a frame 
upon the data that might not fit and thus affect the objective theory. 
However, there furthermore exists a generic set of frames for theo­
retical coding. These are defined by generic properties like econo­
my, process, social ranking etc. Hernandez (2009) define that the 
purpose of the predefined frames are to spark ideas about possible 
interrelations and thus the possible categories within the research 
data. In conclusion should the theoretical codes create the founda­
tion for the later process called theoretical sampling which aim is to 
test and refine the substance of the theoretical codes and the other.
8.5	 Memoing
This is where the researcher actively engage the research data in 
Weave the fractured story back together again.
- Barney G. Glaser
