45
an informal way. It should happen throughout all the three steps of 
coding, and the aim is to write down ideas, concepts, comparisons 
or other relevant thoughts that the researcher might obtain during 
the different stages of coding. It is important do distinguish memo­
ing from focused coding, because focused coding only addresses the 
possible pattern, whereas the memoing scopes everything else that 
the researcher might think of. The result of good memos should be 
text pieces that expand the researchers view on the data and can be 
used later when the theoretical sampling starts. 
Charmaz (2006) define two different techniques to writing mem­
os. These are Clustering and Free Writing. Clustering is a mix of a 
Mind-map and text pieces where one writes freely from the thoughts, 
while visually defining and sub defining the text. This technique is 
useful when trying to figure out the importance or indifference of 
categories and how they are inter connected. Free writing is another 
technique where intense writing is to print every bit of the research­
ers thoughts to the paper. This is comes in handy under the constant 
comparative analysis during the coding, but also under the creation 
of drafts of the theory. The general goal of memoing is just to save 
thoughts and ideas about the data.
8.6	 Theoretical sampling
Charmaz (2006) define this step as where the researcher starts eval­
uating the codes and memos created to the research data. The aim 
is to look for new data that can lead to new aspects, category refine­
ment or category discardment. If new aspects emerges the research­
er must create new codes and memos in order incorporate the new 
aspect discovered. This is a highly iterative and repetitive process 
which is called ‘Theoretical Sampling’ and is shown as the big arrow 
back wards to the data in Figure 19. The theoretical sampling is the 
step in GT where the constant comparative process takes place the 
most because of its repetitive process. The process continue until the 
researcher experiences the point of saturation. At this stage can the 
researcher no longer find new aspects within the data and the cod­
ing and memoing can be said to be saturated with the possible infor­
mation from the research data. The process of theoretical sampling 
furthermore informs the researcher about the quality of the different 
categories created. This can then be used as a strategy to narrow the 
focus into this exact category and thus develop and refine it. Lastly 
should the theoretical sampling process furthermore contribute to 
the findings of the focused coding by developing the understanding 
==PAGE:58==
46
Methodology
of the found pattern and thus making it more refined.
8.7	 Theory Generation
This step is the actual construction of the theory. This is where the 
theory is formulated and put down on paper. The different catego­
ries combines, together with the memos, the framework of the the­
ory and its scope. However, the theory has to relate to the research 
objective and its aspects, since the research objective helps defining 
the overall content and the importance of each category found. This 
defines how much each category should be emphasised and if some 
categories should be discarded. On contrary could it also be that the 
research objective should be redefined based on the new knowledge 
found. It is important that the different categories that make up the 
theory has references back to some of the first codes or participant 
statements since this will give a good and satisfactory transparen­
cy within the theory. The transparency also helps illustrating the 
boundaries for the scope of each category, and is in direct relation 
to the reliability, validity and generalization of each category. These 
three concepts are crucial to constantly evaluate while writing the 
theory and they are explained further in Section 9.3. Finally must 
the researcher evaluate the overall condition of the theory in terms 
of scope, depth, reliability and relevance in order to properly answer 
the complete scope of the research objective.
==PAGE:59==
Interviewing
47
9	 Interviewing
Creating an interview where the participant truly gives an inter-view 
to the researcher is not an easy task and this task becomes even 
more complicated when a researcher chooses not to use any specific 
methodology.  (Kvale, 2007). This is because an interview has a 
specific set of conditions that are unique from case to case and is 
dependent on for example the surrounding atmosphere, the mood 
of the interviewer, the personality of the interviewee etc. Being un­
aware of this often results in scattered and vague data that struggle 
to support the theory which the researcher has built. Therefore, in 
order to avoid this and to create an equal basis for all interviews 
must a researcher be methodically and observant to collect data that 
creates a solid and consistent data set, which later can be analysed 
and bring valid and reliable results to the research. It has, on behalf 
of this, been chosen to use the theory of Kvale (2007) as a frame­
work for creating the interview questions, setting the interview stage 
and working with the data afterwards. The work of Kvale has been 
chosen for this thesis because of his extensive work and world-wide 
recognition in the field of qualitative investigation and data collec­
tion through interviews Kvale (2007).
9.1	 Seven stages of an interview inquiry
Kvale (2007) divides an interview inquiry up into seven different 
stages that, together with the belonging theory for analysis, combine 
the methodology from the moment of formulating the purpose to the 
end of reporting the findings. However, some of the first stages will 
often also be covered by other theories or aspects in a larger assign­
ment like this master thesis. This seven stages are presented below, 
but note that Thematizing, Analysing and Verifying partly has been 
covered by other aspects in this theis, e.g. Grounded Theory in the 
Analysis and Verifying part.
Thematizing - A stage where the researcher formulate the purpose 
of the investigation together with the conceptions that will be 
An interview is an inter-view where knowledge is constructed in the 
interaction between the interviewer and the interviewee.
 - Steinar Kvale 
“
==PAGE:60==
48
Methodology
investigated before in a literature study before the interviews.
Designing - Planning the design of the study. This includes the scope, 
the people to interview, interview-questions etc. This stage is 
executed while being aware of the given aim and eventual mor­
al implications that might be or arise during the interviews.
Interviewing - Conducting the interviews based on an interview 
guide created in the designing stage. 
Transcribing - The stage where the interviews are prepared for the 
later analysis.
Analysing - Applying the desired tools or theories to create results 
that fits the research questions. 
Verifying - Critically evaluating the data and the results against the 
criteria of Validity, Reliability and Generalizability.
Reporting - The final stage where the researcher formulates a type 
of material that communicates the aim, findings, method used 
within the study.
The following sections will only deal with the Designing, Interview­
ing, Transcribing and Verifying part as the rest is under the scope of 
Grounded Theory.
9.2	 Designing and performing interviews
Several aspects needs to be taken into consideration when creating 
the framework for the interviews thought to perform. One must re­
late to aspects like questions to be answered, confidentialities, type 
of person to interview, time available etc. Before the interview will 
the interviewer and the participant have to agree upon the confi­
dentialities for the interview. The position of the participant might 
be an obstacle for the participant talking honest, which means that 
they should have the possibility for being anonymous in the rest of 
the research work. Other aspects like direct quoting or limitations 
in sharing the research work are also important to clear because 
of similar conditions. Lastly is it recommended that the researcher 
performs a short presentation where the participant gets to know 
what the research objective for the research is and how it is planned 
to be achieved.
There are three general types of interview types that can be used 
when interviewing, and each of them has their advantages and disad­
vantages. Kvale (2007) defines them as structured, semi-structured 
and unstructured interviews. Structured interviews are normally 
performed through qualitative surveys and are ideal for reaching 
==PAGE:61==
Interviewing
49
a big amount of people and getting information only about the de­
sired areas, but this is also its disadvantage. The semi-structured in­
terview sets an overall agenda of which topics the interview must 
address, but leaves the exact interview questions as recommended 
for the interview. This allow the participant to address what he/she 
finds important for the topic on the interview agenda and thus cov­
ering aspects that could be unknown for the researcher at that the 
interview time. The unstructured interview form focuses predomi­
nantly on this, but this also means that a researcher potentially has 
to perform a higher amount of interviews, compared to when it is 
semi-structured, in order to obtain a sufficient amount of research 
data. The semi-structured interview has been chosen as the inter­
view-method for this thesis since it offers a balance of gathering data 
and exploring areas unknown to the researcher.
During the interview the interviewer must relate to an interview 
guide. An interview guide is a script that structure the course of an 
interview to a certain degree, dependent on the interview type. It 
is here the researcher has written down topics and questions the 
should be addressed during the interview. It also contains a checklist 
of things that needs to be done before and after an interview. The 
interview guide should be re-designed in order to become unique for 
each interview because the boundary conditions also differs. This 
variation can be caused by several things and the researches amount 
of knowledge is definitely one of them since the previous interview 
has granted more knowledge about the researched area. 
The researcher must furthermore variate the type of questions asked 
to the participant to make the interview a pleasent, variated and 
enriching for both. This means that the researcher must variate be­
tween using two different types of questions. These are called The­
matically question and dynamically questions. The thematically ques­
tions focuses sorely on the purpose of harvesting knowledge to the 
research question, whereas the dynamically questions focuses on the 
stimulation of the topics within the interview. This also includes a 
portion of human interaction to secure or cultivate good interaction 
between interviewer and interviewee. The way the questions can be 
asked also differs, which means that both direct, indirect, probing 
questions can be used to make the interview varied and pleasant. 
Yet the different ways of asking questions can also be used to vali­
date answers from another angle and avoid certain participants to 
answer according to their own agenda. 
See the interview guide cre­
ated for this thesis in Table 
10, on page 58 and 59.
==PAGE:62==
50
Methodology
The research questions should not be used as interview questions 
since they are normally formulated in a academical and theoretical 
language. Interview questions should rather be more simple, tangi­
ble and formulated in an everyday language, since this contributes 
to the natural flow of the interview, but then may require several 
interview questions to cover the whole aspect of an research ques­
tion. This gives, on the other hand, the possibility of approaching the 
research objective from several angles and by that getting a varied 
and rich coverage of the question. It furthermore opens the possibil­
ity of checking possible contradictions between individual interview 
questions. The process of decomposing the main research hypothe­
sis down into the more tangible interview questions is illustrated in 
Figure 20.
When the interview has ended is it good for both the researcher and 
the participant to debrief and summarise what they have been talk­
ing about and what each has learned. This is recommended since 
it is a way of testing the newly gathered knowledge against the re­
search objective. Lastly must the researcher ask if there is anything 
else and thank the participant for participating in the interview. It 
is ideal for the researcher to spend some time after the interview to 
reflect on what has been said and been discussed and to write down 
notes and text parts about key aspects found during the interview.
9.3	 Validation and reliability
A researcher must have, to certain degree, a critical view on the gath­
ered data in order to assess the data through an objective approach 
with the aim of creating trustworthy theories. This assessment must 
be present during the all seven stages of the interview inquiry and 
not as a final step before the theory is finalised. Two terms are nor­
Research objective
Research question
Research question
Research question
Interview question
Interview question
Interview question
Interview question
Interview question
Interview question
Figure 20 - The scale from research hypothesis to actual reasearch questions. In­
spired by Kvale (2007).
==PAGE:63==
Interviewing
51
mally used to check the trustworthiness in qualitative data, and these 
are Reliability and Validity. Kvale (2007) and Carmines and Zeller 
(1979) defines two terms as following:
Reliability refers to the consistency of the gathered data. It is part­
ly measured by the amount of contradictions within the data 
collected. It can also be determined by whether a finding can 
be reproduced from other datasets. The researcher can cross 
check if the participant reply with reliable statements on a sub­
ject by asking questions that prompt the same area from differ­
ent angles during an interview can. The same cross checking 
can also be done between different interviews with different 
participants by going through the material to find statements 
that are similar or equal.
Validity refers to the correctness and strength of the statements in 
the collected data. The way of determining a valid argument or 
statement differ between the interpretive assessment and the 
positivist assessment. Kvale (2007) defines a valid argument 
in the interpretive paradigm as sound, strong, well grounded 
and convincing. These four concepts are one of the only ways 
to test valid arguments in the interpretive paradigm since it 
cannot be validated or corroborated as a statement in the pos­
itivist paradigm. Kvale (2007) defines that a researcher must 
address validation throughout the seven stages of the inter­
view inquiry, and can do it through a method called ‘validity 
as quality of craftsmanship’, which is a three step approach to 
validating. The first step is equal to corroboration. A research­
er has to address statements through its invalidities in order 
to evaluate how strong a statement persists. The second step 
is to question the statement in relation to what needs to be 
answered in the hypothesis, and also why, in order to obtain a 
self critical view to the both the research data and the hypoth­
esis. The third and last step deals with the theory and method 
used to create the theory. The researcher must evaluate the 
methodologies and theories used to obtain the theory. 
Most of the assessments for checking validity are however already 
treated when using grounded theory. This is partly due to the con­
stant comparative assessment seen in Figure 18 on page 41, and 
partly due to the Theoretical sampling as described in Section 8.6. 
Kvale (2007), Charmaz (2006) and Carmines and Zeller (1979) 
deals with this matter to a higher degree in their literature. How­
==PAGE:64==
52
Methodology
ever, this literature is beyond the scope of this thesis and is thus 
not included. This is partly also due to the size and complexity of 
the literature’s subjects like epistemological issues and hermeneutic 
matters etc.
Another important aspect of the validity is the level of which one 
can generalize the valid information. Generalization defines the 
borders of the valid argument from which similar situations or data 
can be said to be valid as well, or support the data that was first 
found. Kvale (2007) defines two possible ways of analysing the gen­
eralization, a statistical way and an analytical way, where each refer 
more or less to the positivist and interpretive assessment. The analyt­
ical generalization involves a reason judgement about the extent to 
which one amount of valid data can be used to evaluate and validate 
other data present in other studies. The next part, part five find, will 
relate to this matter by addressing the amount of contributions to 
the findings. If a finding has received a lot of contributions is can be 
said to have a high level of generalization, whereas findings with few 
contribtutions can be said to only show a tendency, because their 
level of generalization is low. 
==PAGE:65==
Thesis methodology
53
10	Thesis methodology
The previous chapters about Grounded Theory and interviews de­
scribed the essential theory for each aspect of the approach. This 
chapter describes the actual execution of the thought methodology 
of the thesis, starting from the literature study to the theory genera­
tion of the findings.
10.1	 Literature study
The literature study was carried out by the use of a search term in­
dex. The search term index consists of categorised words that can 
be put together to obtain different search terms that, to some degree, 
all are relevant to the aim of the literature study. The different cat­
egories are derived from the aim, method/assessment and tool of 
the thesis objective. The categories and their belonging words are 
seen in Table 9. During the research were these combined to search 
terms such as:
•	 BREEAM decision support tools
•	 LEED early design phase
•	 DGNB and integrated design
The search term index were used on the following databases: Goog­
le Scholar, Web of Science and DTU Findit. This resulted in the 
search within scientific journals as Elsevier,  MDPI, Taylor & Fran­
cis Group along with other archieves containing conference papers, 
master and Ph.D. theses and much more. The size of the searched 
domain has led to the conclusion of having found and evaluated the 
majority of the relevant literature.

10.2	 Participants
Different requirements for the selection of participants were created 
in order to secure a certain amount of reliability and validity in the 
responses from the participants. This resulted in three requirements 
formulated. These were: 
1. Knowledge - participants had to be educated as DGNB auditors in 
office buildings or had to have written a Ph.d. about sustaina­
bility with DGNB as an essential part of the research. This was 
to make sure that the participants had a thorough understand­
ing of the theoretical setup of DGNB, thus securing reliable 
answers for the theory aspect of research objective. 
2. Professional area - there had to be a variation in the participants 
professional area. This requirement were established to secure 
a variation in the responses because of different experience 
with DGNB and its impact on buildings. This would also se­
cure different views on DGNB’s impact on architecture, hence 
more reliable and valid answers to the aspect of architecture 
in the research objective. An optimal distribution would be a 
variation between architects, engineers, contractors and client 
advisors.
3. Experience - the participants had to have experience with a least 
one project where DGNB were implemented, and this should 
either be with the aim of certifying or just with the aim of de­
signing with DGNB. This requirement had to secure that par­
ticipants had enough experience with the design process and 
thus securing reliable answers for the workflow aspect of the 
research objective.
The requirements led to five experts from the Danish building sec­
tion being asked to participate, where four of them agreed to an 
interview. The text below describes participants profiles along with 
the researcher’s profile. The researchers profile has also been de­
scribed due to the aim of creating transparency of how the research­
er’s background has influenced the interviews and the theorising.
Participant 1 - IG
Participant one is a MEP-engineer and project director at a large 
consultancy firm with over 10.000 employees, where the participant 
mostly focuses on client advising. The participant is additionally a 
DGNB auditor in office buildings with many years of experience in 
sustainable building assessment. Lastly is the participant a board 
member of DK GBC and chairman in a one of DK GBC’s commit­
tees. Participant 1 will in the future chapters be refered to as “IG”.
==PAGE:67==
Thesis methodology
55
Participant 2 - JSK
Participant two is an architect in a middle sized architectural com­
pany with about 70 employees. The participant is not an DGNB 
auditor but a DGNB consultant, however, the participant did a Ph.d. 
in sustainability and has through this obtained thorough knowledge 
about the DGNB setup and the LCC plus the LCA calculations. Par­
ticipant two has also been involved in some development teams in 
DK GBC. Participant 2 will in the future chapters be referred to as 
“JSK”. 
Participant 3 - JK
Participant three is an architect and sustainability manager at an 
middle sized architectural company with about 76 employees. The 
participant is a DGNB auditor in office buildings with about 4 years 
of experience in the DGNB framework. Participant three is also a 
teacher in some parts of the DGNB education in DK GBC. Partici­
pant 3 will in the future chapters be referred to as “JK”.
 
Participant 4 - CD
Participant four is a MEP-engineer and sustainability leader in a larg­
er consultancy company. Participant four is a DGNB auditor with 
about three years of experience with sustainability assessments in 
the framework of DGNB, BREEAM and LEED. Participant four has 
also experience in working a construction company. Participant 4 
will in the future chapters be referred to as “CD”.
Researcher 
The researcher has followed the classic education of civil engineer­
ing at the Technical University of Denmark with a focus on façades 
and HVAC-systems. During the studies has the researcher attended 
courses and obtained knowledge in topics like building services, flu­
id mechanics, CFD, indoor climate, transient heat and mass transfer, 
and dynamic indoor climate and energy simulations. The researcher 
has in the final part of the post-graduate education been working as 
a part time engineer with MEP-services and energy calculations.
10.3	 Interviewing and transcribing
The book of Kvale (2007) was used to understand the process of 
an interview. Furthermore added the book valuable inputs to the 
construction of a sound interview guide that would cover all aspects 
==PAGE:68==
56
Methodology
of an interview, such as confidentialities, debriefing etc., and at the 
same time guide the interview in a clear and structured way. The 
interview guide were, as mentioned in the previous chapter, based 
on the idea of a semi-structured interview. This implied that no list 
of questions were to be followed chronologically since this could 
eliminate the chance for the participant to address other important 
areas that the researcher haven’t been aware of when constructing 
the interview guide. The interview guide were only modified slightly 
between the interviews, but the biggest modification were before the 
last interview due to the time between the third and the fourth in­
terview. The modifications were based on specific areas that had to 
be addressed during the interview with the specific participant. The 
process of the interview guide can be seen below together with the 
interview guide questions in Table 10.
---------------------------------------------------------------
Briefing
•	
Introduce the subject, tell about the purpose and aim of work.
•	
Tell about Grounded theory and how the interview will be used 
in the work of this
•	
Clear confidentialities like: Anonymity, Direct Quotation and 
Restrictions to thesis sharing
•	
Questions before the interview
Interview Guide (See Table 10 on page 57)
Debriefing
•	
Summarise on lessons learned to test this against the hypothesis
•	
Anything more to say, thanks for your cooperation, and ask how 
they felt about the interview
---------------------------------------------------------------
The first two interviews were conducted June 23rd, and the third in­
terview were executed the 24th of June. The last interview were exe­
cuted one and a half month later at the 16th of August and were part­
ly used to test some of the findings that had emerged between the 
third and fourth interview. All interviews lasted about an hour but 
was in general kept as short as possible based on a balance between 
theorising time and obtaining an adequate amount of research data. 
The transcribing were done by the researcher that only transcribed 
the knowledge based part and not the social or personal dimensions 
of the interview like laugh, giggles or other human actions between

10.4	 Theorising
The theorising phase are a complicated phase where iterations 
happen numerous times for each of the categories and areas found. 
Figure 21 also seeks to give a summary of how the theorising with 
finding categories based on participants statements has proceeded. 
The figure is therefore also a visual summary of how Appendix E, F 
and G are structured in terms of the key statements and the partic­
ipants contributions etc. The theorising part, represented by num­
ber 4 to 8 in Figure 21, started with the theorising and sampling of 
the first three interviews. The key statements from each interview 
that referred to the same thing were grouped together and thus sup­
porting the a specific area, which is represented by column 4 and 6. 
These emerging areas must furthermore be seen in the relation of 
which aspect of the research objective they fit under in order to get 
a clear separation of the findings and minimise overlap. Especially 
this process of determining the right categories and areas within the 
three different aspects of the research objective were a time consum­
ing process because of its non-linearity and iterative nature, hence 
also the difficulty of illustrating it figure-wise. Furthermore under­
lines this difficulty the importance of doing the theoretical sampling, 
where one go through column 4-8 over and over, until the point of 
saturation. Sometimes was it furthermore necessary to go back to 
the original transcribed text to re-evaluate the codes for the text and 
to see if the text also held other interesting dimensions. Lastly holds 
this process also the evaluation of the findings reliability and validi­
ty since the statements from each participant constantly were cross 
checked with statements from the other participant.
The  first draft led to some findings that were continuous through all 
three interviews and thus corroborated the created categories. This 
led to the last and fourth interview becoming more an interview to 
validate or test some of the categories and findings from the first 
draft. Other areas that the first three participants skipped or failed 
to address were also tested in the last interview with the aim of cov­
ering as many aspects of the topic as possible. The fourth interview 
were then coded and implemented the same way as the first three 
interviews by the use of the theoretical sampling. The findings were 
==PAGE:71==

==PAGE:72==
60
Methodology
in conclusion
Deriving a method for finding decent results for the research ob­
jective is not an easy task, and it quickly becomes a comprehensive 
research in science studies through an evaluation of the different 
research paradigms and their belonging theoretical approaches. The 
interpretive research paradigm showed the most potential for de­
livering sound findings through the use of Grounded Theory in all 
three aspects of the research objective. The findings for the theory 
aspect are expected to be especially interesting. The Grounded The­
ory method is systematic, yet it requires several iterations where a 
researcher constantly has to re-evaluate the findings and their inter­
nal pattern. This makes it a complex and time demanding affair. The 
interview process holds many important aspects that must be ad­
dressed before, during and after an interview, and it is important to 
have created an interview guide for every interview. It is also impor­
tant to clear confidentialities with the participant. The restrictions 
led to interviews with four well qualified participants. This resulted 
in about four hours of total interviewing which has been treated ac­
cording the Grounded Theory.

that is represented in the research objective. It also holds a table

surrounding text.

11	Research findings
Using the grounded theory on the four interviews led to a number 
of different categories emerging within each aspect of the research 
objective defined in Chapter 2. The findings within the three prede­
termined aspects led to a total of 12 categories structured as seen 
in Figure 22. The development of the findings can be seen in Fig­
ure 23 which illustrates the process from raw interview data to the 
coded knowledge within the pre determined categories. The catego­
ries emerged based on the requirement that at least two statements 
supported each other. Some of the categories holds few statements, 
whereas other include at numerous. The size of category also shows 
the certainty of being able to make conclusions on the data. Hence 
some categories can be evaluated to be adequately corroborated by 
statements to hold a conclusion, whereas others only hold enough 
data to show a tendency. 
The structure seen in Figure 23 shows that the interview with Partic­
ipant 4 came in later in the theory development. The interview took 
place about one and a half month after the first three, which means 
that it was also partly used to test and corroborate findings from the 
first three interviews. Each emerged category is described in detail 
throughout the following sections in this chapter.

Findings
11.1	 Theory aspect
The first aspect of the research objective attempts to identify which 
of the 40 criteria that is important to address in terms of attention, 
interrelations or impact on other parts of a building project. 
This aspect used the framework of DGNB to categorise different 
interview responses and statements, and this led to the creation of 
two tables. The first table, which is found in Appendix D on page 
173, categorises every response or statement that relates to any of 
the 40 different criteria from DGNB. Table 11 summarises in short 
which criteria that were addressed in the different interviews. The 
interviews resulted in 26 of the criteria were addressed. This equals 
65% of all 40 criteria and 72% when excluding the site related crite­
ria that only has been mentioned briefly by two of the participants. 
Table 11 also the scarcity of responses within the TEC and SITE cat­
egory. The SITE category were only mentioned briefly in two of the 
four interviews but there were no direct mentioning or evaluation of 
its criteria that could contribute to the research objective. Filtering 
out some of the criteria that did not meet the required number of 
responses, and did not have any significant information, led to the 
five areas within the theory aspect as seen in Table 12. The five areas 
reflects the main criteria categories from DGNB, which is also seen 
in Figure 22.
General
A general finding was that all four participants stated that the client 
had a significant impact on which criteria that was found important 
when designing for a high level of DGNB. The reasoning were that 
each client had a different focus and that the main purpose for the 
client to construct a larger office building were to get a building tai­
lored to their needs. One participant answered: 
However, the responses from the participants also showed that some 
criteria were more important than others, regardless of the client 
desires, and these are addressed in the following sub-sections. 
My opinion is that the certification must bring value to the client that 
has ordered it. This means that I, regardless of the difficulty, must 
strive to fulfil the criteria related to indoor climate and degassing 
from materials, if a client has a specific focus on these.
- CD

Findings
Environment category
The environmental group was found to have a big importance for all 
participants with a special focus on two criteria ENV 1.1 - LCA and 
ENV 1.2 - Local Environmental Impact. Table 13 summarises the 
key findings through statements where the “x” marks the contribu­
tion from the participants to each statement. 
Three participants that responded to the Life Cycle Assessment (LCA) 
stated that it was an important criteria to address due to its size of 
8% within DGNB (see Figure 12 on page 22 for size comparison), 
hence the possible number of DGNB credits achievable, and also 
due to its complexity and impact on many other parameters of a 
building such as the materials/products used, the structural system 
and energy usage etc. The time spend on the LCA were also pointed 
out as being a factor that contributed to its importance. The material 
quantities were defined as being one of the dominant factors since it 
could take long time to decompose the BIM model, however, other 
also addressed the issue of retrieving the correct EPD’s to avoid us­
ing generic data from Ökobau for the LCIA as being time consuming. 
The participants advocated for addressing the LCA in bits through­
out the design process while also varying in the level of detail. They 
e.g. suggested a qualitative assessment in the very first phases in 
order to optimise the work with the LCA and also to let it become 
an active design tool rather than an evaluation tool.

==PAGE:81==
Research findings
69
Two Participants addressed another facet of the LCA. They found it 
difficult to optimise the LCA score by tweaking the material usage 
since the energy usage and energy production had a much higher im­
pact in the overall calculation and thus favoured buildings with much 
embodied energy and little energy usage rather than buildings with 
a low embodied energy and higher energy usage. This eliminated 
the motivation for designing with timber instead of brick, or similar, 
since the PV-panels became the dominant factor. Lastly, one partici­
pant advocated for allowing, to some degree, for over-performing to 
compensate for other under-performing areas due to DGNB and the 
Danish law being too rigid in the current situation.
The Local Environmental Impact, which is criterion ENV 1.2, were 
defined as being the most time consuming criterion compared to its 
size by all four participants, which elaborated that the process was 
simple but the documentation were the issue and that it was a “all or 
nothing” criterion meaning that it was impossible to achieve if just 
one document were missing in the documentation. The gathering of 
the documents also scoped from the schematic design phase to the 
construction phase, which meant a high risk of loosing documents, 
like packing slips, due to other involved parties being unaware of 
this matter. Another facet was that limitations to the materials had 
to be incorporated in the contract in order to achieve this criterion. 
This resulted in a higher price from the contractor due to the lim­
itations on contractors choice of material. This composition made 
it economically expensive, and resulted in some of the participants 
experiencing a design team in a project discarding the criterion, and 
sometimes even in the very start. 
Economical category
The participants primarily responded or mentioned the Life Cycle 
Economy (LCC) within DGNB’s economical category. The key find­
ings from this category can be found in Table 14. The LCC was also 
found to be one of the most important criterion within the DGNB 
assessment, and it was furthermore, together with the LCA, one of 
the first criterion to be mentioned by all four participants during the 
interviews. Three out of four participants contributed to the first 
statement within Table 14 which leads to the conclusion of it being 
highly important. One participant mentioned the LCC as a criterion 
that normally did not get the same amount of attention, even though 
some clients were interested in the LCC calculations. This was ex­
plained with the client having their focus on traditional areas such as 
indoor climate or architecture. 
 The purpose of ENV 1.2 - 
Local Environmental Impact 
is to minimise the risk of 
impact from hazardous 
materials on the human and 
the local environment
==PAGE:82==
70
Findings
The LCC was not found difficult to work with, however, it was found 
time consuming due to the many stakeholders within the project 
along with all the interrelations to e.g. energy usage, glazed areas, 
architectural details etc. 
All four participants contributed to the statement about the LCC 
being a valuable tool for many facets like architecture, passive en­
ergy design etc. because it, as one participant defined it, expanded 
the economic horizon for all stakeholders in the design team. One 
participant had the following response to the b
enefits of LCC:
The desire from the client did, however, have a big impact on the 
result of the LCC. This is was due to different economical desires 
depending on whether the client was an investment company, tra­
ditional business or a governmental organisation. The investment 
company were defined by the participants to have a limited econom­
ical horizon that affected the long term view and only optimised the 
initial cost. This is in contrast with the governmental organisation 
that were defined as being interested in the long term view and thus 
keeping the initial cost down. Despite this argued some of the partic­
ipants that an LCC and DGNB often raised the quality of the project 
in general due to the many aspects that had to be included and also 
due to the knock out criteria for each DGNB level e.g. DGNB Gold 
or DGNB platinum.

71
Social category
The social category were the one category that the participants var­
ied the most according to the desires from the client. Two partici­
pants defined a company’s CSR-policy to be an element determining 
which criteria that were to receive the most attention. One partici­
pant defined this by:
The key findings can be seen in Table 15. A general finding was that 
most of the indoor climate aspects are already in focus from most 
of the stakeholders. This was explained by one participant with the 
indoor climate being connected to the employees productivity and 
thus the company’s economical result. However, other participants 
also mentioned that ambitious indoor climate requirements had a 
negative impact on the LCC due to larger installations and more 
BMS, which resulted in larger initial and running costs. 
All four participants noted that the social category had a lot of cri­
teria that varied in size, importance and awareness from a design 
team. Safety and Security, Design for All and Cyclist Facilities were 
mentioned when asked for criteria that were easy to implement and 
had a positive effect on the total level of sustainability. These criteria

attention during the interview, partly because of the aspect within 
the research objective, but also because there exists disharmony in 
DGNB and its measurement of architectural quality. This facet will 
be addressed to a greater detail in Section 11.3.
Technical category
Table 16 shows the scarce response to the technical category. This 
were in general not mentioned during the interviews, and when the 
participants was about it, they stated that most of what was within 
the technical category already were implemented during a normal 
design process, and this was either due to the project brief or the 
building regulation. An example were given by a participant that 
said the building envelope usually achieved a maximum in DGNB 
due to ambitious requirements often following when certifying a 
building with DGNB. There existed some issues with some of the 
criteria, however, these issues were known to DK GBC that worked 
on making the criteria less inconvenient.
Process category
This process category has many similarities with the theory aspect 
found in Section 11.2 due to it treating the same subject but from dif­
ferent angles. This part scopes the more DGNB specific actions that 
is suggested, rather than a more general evaluation of the workflow, 
which takes place in Section 11.2. 
All four participants found the process category in DGNB very use­
ful through all phases of a design process. They generally found it 
quite comprehensive and time consuming, but when asked if it was 
worth the effort they all agreed. One participant defined it as an el­
ement that created security for quality, and that every team member 
could benefit from it. The participants also focused on the Contract 
and tender phase element of the process. They all argued that a con­
tract or project brief had to include specific requirements to parts of 
DGNB, such as the 40 different criteria, to secure common under­
standing of the approach and an equal level of ambition. The partici­

pants mentioned the core criteria from the client as a recommended 
start, but also stated that specific requirements to the calculation 
methods of LCC also was important, even when DGNB was already 
a requirement. Additionally mentioned two of the participants that it 
was important to implement an approach in the project brief of how 
the team should obtain the rest of the DGNB points to achieve the 
desired DGNB score, when having defined the clients core criteria. 
They found it important since experience showed that the cheapest 
solution often were the one chosen when not having defined any 
approach. However, they also emphasised that the cheapest solution 
was not always considered negative since some clients favoured this 
consequence and still obtained a more sustainable building when 
the minimum of money spend. 
In general, many of these elements could be traced back to the crea­
tion of the project brief, and three participants stated that the work 
of the client advisor during the creation of the project brief had a 
significant influence on the end result. Thus the participants stat­
ed that the client advisor had to pay attention to implementing the 
DGNB requirements. Another facet of the process category was the 
design process where three out of four participants found it impor­
tant to create dsign concepts for areas that were not normally seen

as important in the start. They stated that concepts for energy, ven­
tilation, structure etc. came by it self by during the first phases and 
that a team should address other parts of a building to a higher de­
gree. The participants linked this approach with criterion PRO 1.3 
and underlined the importance of this. Some of the participant also 
noted to this, that the use of renewables, as mentioned before, gave 
more freedom in almost all other concepts, thus making the creation 
of design concepts easier and with a higher possible design freedom. 
Lastly, two out of the four participants addressed the fact that having 
a simple and sound proposal in every concept made it much more 
tangible for the client and the design team and thus better collabo­
ration.
Lastly one participant found the commissioning criterion, PRO 2.3, 
essential for the final outcome of the whole process. The participant 
defined it as being a check for what has been designed also has been 
produced. The same participant also stated that the commissioning 
phase in other BSATs such as LEED and BREEAM had a much high­
er importance and advocated projects for adapting this importance. 
Sub conclusion for theory
All participants agreed to the client having a big impact of what is 
seen as important for the specific building to be designed since the 
building must add maximum value for the end user. The LCA and 
the LCC were both found as key criteria of DGNB due to their size 
and relations to other parts of a building, and it was recommended 
to work with them in a stepwise approach, where the level of detail 
increases along with the project. The LCC were also found to often 
support the architectural solutions because it connected architecture 
with a life time money perspective. The local environmental impact 
was found to be a considerable obstacle due to its documentation 
requirements. The social category could have the greatest variation 
criteria that was found important by the client. The CSR-policy were 
a good determination of which DGNB-criteria that the client found 
important and should choose as key criteria. The design team and 
the client should also set an approach for how the rest of the DGNB 
points should be achieved. A project brief had to include specific re­
quirements to DGNB parts, such as the different criteria, to secure a 
common ground for approach and level of ambition for all stakehold­
ers in the project. Lastly there had to be created early concepts for 
all aspects of the building, especially for the area not usually thought 
of, plus commissioning was good to incorporate and execute due to 
its aim of giving the client what has been thought.
==PAGE:87==
Research findings
75
11.2	 Workflow aspect
This second aspect of the research objective attempts to identify how 
a design team should work when designing a building with a high 
DGNB score. This workflow should include responses to ideal col­
laboration and the correct approach to the different DGNB criteria.
Four different areas emerged when sampling for the workflow and 
these are seen in the Figure 25. These areas found holds the cat­
egories with different findings. The full sampling can be found in 
Appendix F on page 185, and Table 18 summarises in short, which 
categories that emerged during the theoretical sampling. As men­
tioned earlier, would this aspect potentially hold some overlaps with 
the theory aspect and its process category. The following text there­
fore tries to illustrate how these areas scopes differently from the 
category in the theory aspect. 
Contract setup - this area gathers the responses from the participants 
that touches the contract and how a contract should be built to 
embrace a good workflow.
Collaboration - this area focuses on specific responses on how a  team 
should collaborate during the design of a building. 
Approach - this addresses the responses that relates to what and how 
to approach certain aspect of the building in the given situations. 
Documentation - this gathers the responses there has been from the 
participants about the documentation that DGNB requires.
General
The collaboration in projects where DGNB was implemented turned 
out to be similar to any other collaboration in other projects where 
the mindset of integrated design are used. Participant one, who was 
a board member of DK GBC, also noted this might be because the 
DGNB process category were designed and optimised to follow the 
design process. However, some specific deviations appeared within 
some of the following categories.

Contract setup
The general requirements for the workflow had to include specific re­
quirements, optimally specific criteria from DGNB, for the securing 
a sound contract. It also had to include what to expect in order to 
cultivate the collaboration and the design process. If performed well 
it motivated the team for completing a DGNB certification. This was 
a point all participants were found to agree to. 
Collaboration
Table 20 show the responses for the collaboration category. The 
optimal design process when designing with DGNB were found to 
be similar to any other project following the principles of an Inte­
grated Design Process. When the participants were asked what they 
thought of the eco-charrette implemented in LEED, they responded 
it was similar to any other meeting where expectations are aligned 
during an Integrated Design Process. Despite this did the partici­
pants find it important for the team to understand the exact scope of 
work related to DGNB and also the required steps to get there. But 
the participant also noted that this was more a general experience 
they had in both normal projects and DGNB projects.
A more specific element for DGNB was the scope of work for the 
DGNB assessor. Two of the four participants noted the importance 
of understanding the difference between a project manager and the 
process manager (DGNB assessor), and how their scope of work 
differed from each other. The work of both could not be handled by 
one person due to the amount of work. The result would be parts 
lacking focus and thus not achieving the requirements. The partic­
ipants noted to this project manager and the DGNB assessor still 
had to work closely together even if they had clear a boundary for 
the scope of work. The DGNB assessor should focus sorely on the 
communication between the stakeholders in terms of DGNB, along 
with creating clear communication of the required documentation. 
One participant did additionally mention that the scope of work for 
the DGNB assessor could vary significantly due to the different ap­
proaches to how certification were performed, and gave an example 
of certifying “as built”, but most of the participants did mention the

participant mentioned that a DGNB assessor’s previous experience 
had a big impact on the project outcome.
Another dimension of the workflow and the project are the stake­
holders. Two of the participants mentioned that the result of making 
all concepts criteria tangible were certain areas became ‘locked’ or 
too defined. This had the consequence of limiting the space of solu­
tions for the architect to create a design. But this is treated later 
in the research aspect about architecture. One participant’s advice 
to this issue was to focus on giving the architect the space needed 
to create good architecture. This can be related to another finding 
about stakeholders. One participant noted that different stakehold­
ers naturally had different interests as in any other project, however, 
the specific interests from every stakeholder were possible to place 
in one of the three legs of sustainability. Examples were given with 
a contractor and an architect, where the contractor were defined as 
having focus on the economical and the social parts of sustainability 
and the architects having their focus of social and environmental.

were defined and followed right from the start. Doing this resulted

pants that a team should maintain a holistic perspective and avoid a 
sub-optimising certain criteria because it could give more points in 
DGNB. Doing this sub-optimisation could lead to a team spending 
too much money and too much time on achieving a certain score 
in e.g. indoor climate, resulting in the team having little time and 
little fund left for some of the other issues. Two of the participants 
mentioned that some criteria, which would normally not get that 
much attention, should be drawn into the light or into focus since 
they could hold a considerable value for the project. Participant one 
used here the example of accessibility and underlined how impor­
tant it could be for e.g. people being blind. This also had an econom­
ical advantage later if the client wanted to sell the building since it 
can embrace a bigger segment. Lastly should bigger criteria, such 
as the LCA and the LCC, be addressed step-wise throughout the de­
sign phases because of their complexity and interference with other 
changing criteria. Furthermore do they hold a big potential amount 
of DGNB points.
The dimension of costs and price savings also emerged during the 
interviews. Some participants had experience with using LCC as an 
active design tool through the design process, and this had often re­
sulted in a cheaper project on the long run due the increased focus 
running cost. The type of client did, however, also have a significant 
impact on the possible price savings, as mentioned earlier under the 
LCC category in the Theory aspect. Participant four also had some 
experience with contractors and mentioned that certain require­
ments to e.g. materials would lead increased prices from the con­
tractor. This was because a contractor often aimed for price tagging 
each criteria in a project and specific requirements prevented them 
using their traditional supply chain, and this resulted in them having 
to find another supplier to a higher price. Lastly, one participant 
focussed on the results of price saving when having a certain DGNB 
criteria. It was argued that getting a certified building as cheap as 
possible was not necessarily a bad thing since the economical aspect 
of sustainability were maximised in that assessment. As the partici­
pant defined it:
And the alternative could be a equally sustainable building, but more 
expensive.
So even if the approach is “as cheaply as possible” have you still 
lifted the project and building at many different levels.
- CD
“
==PAGE:92==
80
Findings
Documentation
The amount of documentation were mentioned by some of the partic­
ipants as a influencing factor on the project. Three participants found 
that DGNB increased the amount of documentation compared to a 
traditional project, yet the requirements were found reasonable since 
much of the documentation were made anyway, it just needed to be 
formalised. A part of the participants had previously met challenges in 
a team, which was a consequence of the increased level of documenta­
tion. Some team members were generally against the extra work and 
documentation required, because they failed to understand the pur­
pose of the work. The same participant that advocated for spending 
time on the process category in the early stage, defined that this could 
be solved by everyone’s understanding of the DGNB process require­
ments. Lastly one participant mentioned that the increased level of 
documentation were difficult to produce with the same salary since 
the team delivered more and had to work longer for the same thing.
Sub Conclusion for workflow
The general setup for both the contract and the design process were 
found to be similar to any other project following the integrated design 
process, but it had to include specific requirements taken from DGNB 
to secure good implementation. The tasks of a project manager and 
a DGNB assessor could not be handled by one person, so the field of 
work should have a clear boundary. However, the two of them still 
had to work closely together to manage communication, aims and re­
quired documentation. The early stage assessment with DGNB were 
recommended to be made through a qualitative assessment touching 
all the DGNB criteria, and especially the DGNB process criteria. The 
general assessment should be taken in steps with a holistic perspective 
to avoid a sub-optimisation of criteria. The increased level of docu­
mentation were a challenge for teams, but the participants found it 
reasonable due to the documentation being created anyway.

11.3	 Architecture aspect
The third and last aspect of the research objective attempts to iden­
tify which influence the DGNB certification system has on the ar­
chitecture - if a certain pattern can be found in the quality or the 
typology etc. 
Three different areas emerged during the sampling of the interviews 
and are illustrated in Figure 26. The full sampling for the architec­
tural aspect can be found in Appendix G on page 191. The areas 
holds some categories that emerged during the theoretical sampling, 
and these can be found in a summarised versoin in Table 23. The 
following text will shortly clarify the three different areas that the 
sampling led to:
Architectural quality - this area scopes the responses from the par­
ticipants where the quality of the architectural solutions, in all 
scales, has been mentioned. 
Architectural Traits - this area gathers the responses that the par­
ticipants gave when asked about DGNB’s influence on the ar­
chitectural traits that the architect created. 
Diamond - this area holds responses about an upcoming initiative 
from DGNB called “Diamond”, which was mentioned by all 
the participants.
 
Architectural Quality
The architectural quality became a dominant element of the inter­
view with all four participants when asked which impacts DGNB 
had on the architecture. Again, as mentioned before, the LCC were 
pulled out as an example since the tool supported the use of sound 
and robust materials on the building envelope leading to an expres­
sion of quality. Another facet of the quality was the joint solutions 
or the assemby of the building. One participant mentioned that the 
requirements for design for disassembly in DGNB led to some pro­
jects acutally having technical solutions that was simpler and easier

evaluates the projects in the competions on an overall evaluation 
level. But architecture in general lacked presence in DGNB in terms 
of explicit evaluation and points given for good architecture. This 
lack were seen as a considerable shortage of DGNB and its quality 
measure by several of the participant, however, DK GBC and DGNB 
were currently developing a solution to this, which is discussed in 
the Diamond Area. The consequence would be buildings that has a 
high certification level in DGNB, i.e. gold or platinum, but lacked

which is also similar to responses given by the other participant. 
Lastly, one participant mentioned that the SAVE-method could be 
could be used simultaneously with DGNB to meet the desire for 
a more explicit evaluation of the architectural quality. In any case, 
all the participants were aware of the previous cases with German 
buildings having a high score in DGNB that lacked architectural val­
ue. The participants used words as ‘horrible’, ‘too simple’ and ‘ba­
nal’ to descirbe the buildings and their architectural value designed 
in the early days of DGNB, and said that the industry had learned 
from those cases since. The participants mentioned that this now 
meant the architect were given ‘space’ enough to design buildings 
that were better than these.
Architectural Traits
When the participants were asked about architectural traits they 
were also asked if they saw the same tendency of traits present for 
engineering when working with DGNB. The responses were gener­
ally that this was not existing. The participants noted that the engi­
neering companies generally had a more company specific profile 
that determined their approach, rather than a specific project. Exam­
ples were given where it was stated that one engineering company 
focused on producing energy rather than reducing it, and another 
company focused on reducing specific areas such as ventilation.
Three of the participants agreed to the statement of DGNB influ­
encing the specific architectural solutions negatively, with a varying 
degree. One participant noted that the original intention of DGNB 
were to avoid defining prescriptive solutions, like BREEAM and 
LEED had a big tendency to, but instead creating a frame with per­
... it may just as well be a sustainable building, but if it is an ugly 
building, will it not be there for very long.

formance based requirements that gives the design team a higher 
freedom to develop a fitting solution to the project. But this freedom 
varies in the different categories of DGNB and the social category 
were the one with the lowest share of performance based criteria, 
thus placing the highest amount of specific requirements/solutions 
to the design. The same participant advocated, based on this, for a 
revision of the criteria that a more prescriptive approach. 
The specific consequences mentioned by the participants to this is­
sue were for example the requirement about the number of orien­
tations out of the building. Another participant also mentioned the 
issue about producing or saving energy for a building in LCA and 
addressed that the PV-panels and concrete structure scored higher 
than a wooden building with a concept of minimal embodied energy. 
Two participants also outlined that the extensive use of the passive 
design methods also had a big impact on the buildings since it creat­
ed an monolithic mass, or identical typology, but this had little to do 
with DGNB setup itself. Lastly, when asked what impact this specific 
requirements had on themselves and their work they replied that 
they tweaked their mindset or their working routine to draw it as de­
fined in DGNB since this eliminated the problem and they avoided 
struggling with it later in the design process. 
Diamond 
Every participant answered yes to the question about DGNB influ­
ences the architectural traits and the architecture having too little 
influence on the criteria also mentioned the new initiative from 
DGNB. This initiative is called Diamond and it seeks to evaluate the 
architectural solution only, through a qualitative way, thus making 
it contrary to the formula based setup of DGNB. The categories are 
called “first impression ...” because the Diamond initiative are still 
on the drawing board, which therefore means that none of the inter­
viewed participants had any experiences with it and were only able 
to comment on the general idea and aim of it.

All four participants liked and acknowledged the general aim of the 
Diamond initiative, and some participants said that it definitely was 
needed. The first impression advantages was that all participants saw 
a potential in it and that the initiative could give the architectur­
al aspect in DGNB the needed lift of importance. Additionally the 
participants liked the approach since seeks to evaluate in a holistic 
and qualitative way, and this is also in favour of the architecture. 
The first impression weaknesses was that Diamond lacked integra­
tion with the rest of the DGNB and that its approach were different 
compared to DGNB’s formula based setup. Some participants were 
also sceptical of the possible outcome, since architecture is subjec­
tive and that ‘ugly’ buildings still could receive the diamond tag. One 
participant asked why DGNB could not just trust the design team, 
but wanted to see the initiative in use before drawing a conclusion.
Sub conclusion for architecture
Two participants stressed that the type of contract had an impact 
on the quality, but that DGNB usually helped with underlining the 
importance of some aspect due to its criteria requirements, except 
for architecture. The participants were aware of the German exam­
ples that lacked architectural value and lessons were learned from it. 
Three of the participants agreed to the statement of DGNB influenc­
ing the specific architectural solutions negatively in a varying degree. 
This made them tweak their mindset for working so they obtained a 
routine to draw it as defined in DGNB to comply with DGNB. All 
saw a potential in Diamond and thought it could give the architec­
tural aspect of DGNB the needed lift of importance, however they 
found that Diamond lacked integration with the rest of the DGNB 
and that its approach were different compared to DGNB’s formula 
based approach.

This part evaluates the findings through a classic structure of a dis­
cussion, conclusion and possible future research. It takes a look at 
the findings from the previous part and evaluates them against other 
studies, and conclusively evaluates them against the research objec­
tive of this thesis.
DISCUSSION & CONCLUSION
Part V
==PAGE:102==
An endless number of green buildings 
doesn’t make a sustainable city.
Bjarke Ingels
“
==PAGE:103==
Discussion
91
12	Discussion
This part will discuss the findings from the four interviews and put 
them in relation to the research objective and at the same time eval­
uate the findings validity by comparing them to the literature from 
the Part II - Background.
It becomes clear by looking at the findings in the previous part that 
the four interviews held a big amount of knowledge which the par­
ticipants has gathered through a many years of experience. To this 
must it therefore be noted that some knowledge found has been left 
out from the findings since it either lacked a category, were highly 
specific or were not in relation to the research objective. This knowl­
edge is only present in transcribed interview text and can be found 
in Appendix A, B, C and H. Furthermore summarises Figure 27 on 
page 87 the findings and it seeks to illustrate some of the key 
findings in each of the three aspects in accordance with the research 
objective. The figure holds maximum three key findings from each 
category investigated. 
12.1	 General observations
The big figure, Figure 27 on page 87, that summarises the key find­
ings shows a relatively uniform amount of responses for each of the 
three aspects. The participants had surprisingly uniform statements, 
which means that the general level of reliability can be considered as 
good in almost all categories. Those categories with little responses, 
or only one person contributing to the statement, are evaluated to 
only show a tendency rather than a finding. 
Studying the findings, which has been found using the Grounded 
Theory, against the background literature found in Part II - Back­
ground leads to interesting observations. As evaluated in Chapter 7 
had the interpretive paradigm different advantages and disadvantag­
es compared to the positivist paradigm because of the fundamental 
difference in the assessment and evaluation. This difference is seen 
when one evaluate the findings in the theory aspect of this study with 
findings from the use of MCDM. Taking the example of Kreiner, Pas­
ser and Wallbaum (2015) they find that some criteria are more im­
portant than other and that the LCA and LCC definitely are impor­
==PAGE:104==
92
Discussion & Conclusion
tant because of their quantitative large active and passive influence. 
However, the method used does not give any other description of 
why, besides the large interdependency and quantitative size. Using 
Grounded Theory leads to the same main findings, but instead with 
mere descriptions of why they are important and how it should be 
tackled, but no quantification comparison. Thus, it is clear by look­
ing at the two results that they conclude the same and support the 
same findings in different ways. This leads to an overall conclusion 
which can be argued to be stronger and more thorough. This is also 
similar to what Schweber (2016) argues and leaves the reader with 
a broader understanding of the field. However, this greater under­
standing only valid for the areas where the findings using the two 
paradigms overlap, and this limits the scope to the theory aspect of 
the thesis findings.
12.2	 Theory aspect
The participants had surprisingly few specific comments to the ener­
gy demand and energy calculations. Some of them did mention that 
this area already was of high importance for clients and the build­
ing sector due to the building regulation’s strict requirements. This 
can explain why one participant described the energy assessment 
as “shallow to work with”, yet the same participant also mentioned 
that a general perception of a sustainable assessment were put in 
relation to a low energy usage. This means that high requirements 
for energy were already defined in a project brief when a sustainable 
assessment was in focus. Another reason to this might also be that 
the energy assessment is scattered within DGNB and thus struggles 
getting mentioned when asked about specific criteria in DGNB.
Another interesting point is that the findings suggest to do a stepwise/
iterative approach for the LCA throughout the different building 
phases. This makes it important to relate to the findings of Takano 
and Kuittinen (2013), and utilise these finding to obtain qualified es­
timates of the LCA-results earlier in the design process. One study is 
of cause not enough to do qualified estimates on advanced LCA-cal­
culations, but it generally shows a demand in the building sector and 
thus a need to investigate the area further. Lastly should it be noted 
that the results of Kreiner, Passer and Wallbaum (2015) showed that 
LCA has one of the largest active influences and the biggest passive 
influence, leaving it as one of the key criterion to address in the 
ÖGNI version of DGNB.

93
Another observation is that the technical category only received 
about three responses in total during four interviews of about an 
hour each, as seen in Appendix D. One participant argues to this 
that the Danish building regulation already sets good standards for 
this area, and this makes the technical category loose importance 
and focus in a project. If this is true could it be argued that the 
requirements should be more strict in order to bring the technical 
category into focus. Yet, if the technical requirements can be con­
sidered high already, should the spotlight instead shift to evaluate 
other aspects that require the focus. This is, however, only based on 
a quite limited amount of information, and to be able to conclude, or 
even show a tendency, should more data about this area be gathered. 
 
Lastly, in the process category, was it found that the contract should 
contain a clear set of requirements to the client’s core criteria. But 
some participants underlined that a design team also had to make 
an agreement of where the rest of the DGNB points should be found, 
in order to obtain the desired certification level, e.g. DGNB Gold. 
This was because the core criteria not was enough to obtain the 
desired certification level, simply because they were too few. The 
participants said that the rest of the points either could be found in 
a “cheapest possible” way, or by other ways such as indoor climate, 
environmental impact or a holistic approach where parameters from 
all the five main categories were addressed. Hence, the approach 
became a two-step approach where the last step would be to define a 
general aim for where the rest of the points should be found, and this 
had to be done together with the client, so the team and the client 
could work out the best solution for the given project. This two step

approach is illustrated in Figure 29 and can be defined as a steering 
tool that can be used to clarify a common aim for the design team 
and furthermore easily communicating the aim to all team members, 
and thus reducing possible problems in the collaboration.
12.3	 Workflow aspect
The findings show that the Integrated Design Process (IDP) changed 
slightly when applying a DGNB, compared to a traditional project. 
Only few specific things were drawn into the light by the participants 
and examples were the contract setup or the collaboration between a 
project manager and a DGNB manager were mentioned. This is also 
in line with what was found in the literature found and described in 
the background study. Here, both Bruunsgaard and Larsen (2015) 
and Schweber and Haroglu (2014) concluded that a BSAT “just 
adds another layer to the project”. Furthermore can parallels also 
be found between the statement about understanding the design pro­
cess and the findings of Schweber and Haroglu (2014) that mention 
every person in the team must understand the design process.
Contract related findings showed that the specific requirements from 
the DGNB scheme to the workflow aspect and the theory aspect 
should be incorporated into the contract to make legal bindings, so 
called fetters, to secure a proper foundation for a design team. This 
is supported by the findings of Kreiner, Passer and Wallbaum (2015) 
that showed criterion such as Integral planning and Quality of pro­
ject preparation in the Austrian adoption of DGNB scored high in 
their active influence and little in the passive influence, meaning they 
were fit to steer the project. See Figure 15 on page 31. This might 
be self-explanatory, and the underlying thoughts of the investigation 
made by Kreiner, Passer and Wallbaum is not known either, but the 
findings support and corroborate each other. 
As mentioned in the findings part were the participants asked about 
the eco-charrette term from LEED, and they responded they thought 
it was the same as balancing expectations, however, no participants 
addressed the specific similarity between this term and what DGNB 
has implemented to balance expectations. One participant did un­
derline the importance of criteria PRO 1.3, see Table 2 on page 
23 for DGNB table, which is about concept creation and making 
the general concept tangible and understandable for the client. The 
aim of this criteria relatively similar to the aim on an eco-charrette 
since both seeks to balance expectations by setting specific goals 
and creating concepts. It also has similarities with the pre-certifica­
==PAGE:107==
Discussion
95
tion workshop that is possible to perform in DGNB. It can the be 
concluded, if these elements truly are similar, that the creation of 
concepts and the balancing of expectations is seen as in a universal 
matter and that it corroborates the importance of executing such 
meetings or workshops for the cultivation and steering the project. 
In relation to the increased documentation requirements and the 
resistance met, is it interesting to observe that issues still are present, 
even though DGNB has created the process category to integrate 
the certification as seamlessly as possible. The same trend has also 
been observed by Schweber (2013) who found that project manag­
ers working with BREEAM noted difficulties in getting some team 
members to produce the required documentation. However, if the 
issue is that people still find the required documentation time de­
manding and troubling, even if the DGNB states that 80% of the 
documentation has to be made anyway as stated by Ebert, Eißig & 
Hauser (2011), might it be that the issue lies within the way of requir­
ing the final and formalised documentation. Thus, overcoming this 
issue in the future might be done by mapping the formal required 
way of delivering all documentation, so that it is explicitly illustrated 
for the design team and furthermore implemented in the contract 
from the very start. Lastly is it important to note that DGNB are a 
voluntary element with no relation to Danish building regulations, 
and because of this will some team members might oppose the extra 
requirements to documentation, since it gives more work and it is 
not a law requirement.
Lastly, to summarise the workflow, illustrates Figure 30 on page 
96 an attempt to apply the workflow findings to an Integrated 
Design Process described by Löhnert, Dalkowski and Sutter (2003). 
This attempt has been made within this discussion part in stead of 
the Part IV - Findings due to it being a interpretation of the results, 
which therefore also holds insecurity about the findings having a cor­
rect placement within the integrated design process. To make this 
figure a part of the findings would, according to the methodology 
and theory, require additionally interviews where the participants 
were prompted with the figure, in order to evaluate the validity and 
possible changes to the findings. The figure shows five phases called 
Project brief, Pre design, Concept design, Detailed design and Con­
struction. At first should the building programme/project brief be 
filled with the relevant requirements to core criteria from the client 
along with different requirements to the workflow. In this phase is it 
important to have a client councillor that knows DGNB so a sound 
==PAGE:108==
96
Discussion & Conclusion
and pro-active project brief can be created. The next phase is the 
pre-design phase. This is where the design team evaluate expecta­
tions and set common agreements for things like documentation and 
process. The design team should in also evaluate the core criteria 
along with the approach for the remaining criteria in order to obtain 
a common concord with the client. Another important element is 
that the project should be evaluated through a qualitative approach 
with DGNB, since this resulted in a good start for the DGNB as­
sessment of the project. Lastly is it important for this phase that all 
members of the design team understand the entire design process 
for the project, because this could potentially minimise future prob­
lems. The concept design phase should focus on creating sound and 
tangible concepts for the different parts of the building. It is here im­
portant to include areas not normally thought of in order to create a 
better and more thorough concept. The bigger criteria that has a big 
impact on the design should here start to be addressed in an iterative 
approach. Lastly is it important for the design team to understand 
the need for “space” for architecture to have a good foundation for 
the architectural quality. The detailed design has a high level of de­
tail, but the design team should avoid loosing a holistic perspective 
to the DGNB criteria. Furthermore should the bigger criteria again 
undergo an evaluation. It it here important to again stress that archi­
tecture cannot be too locked, by numerous different requirements 
and criteria, since it can lead to critical loss of architectural quality. 
Lastly is it important in this phase for the assessor and the project 
manager to stress the required documentation for the design team 
and what has been agreed upon in the previous phases. The con­
struction does not have much, but the team should remember the 
importance of commissioning.
